1
Set and Map
1.1
Set and Element
Sets and elements are the most basic concepts of mathematics. Given any element x and any
set X, either x belongs to X (denoted x ∈X), or x does not belong to X (denoted x ̸∈X).
A set can be presented by listing all its elements.
Example 1.1
• X1 = {1, 2, 3, · · · , n} is the set of all integers between 1 and n;
• X2 = {2, −5} is the set of all numbers satisfying the equation x2 + 3x −10 = 0.
Example 1.2
• Y1 = {a, b, c, · · · , x, y, z} is the set of all latin alphabets;
• Y2 = {red, green, blue} is the set of basic colours (whose combinations form all the other colours);
• Y3 = {red, yellow} is the set of colours in the Chinese national ﬂag;
• The set Y4 of all registered students in this class is the list of names the registration oﬃce gave to me.
A set can also be presented by describing the properties its elements satisfy.
Example 1.3
• Natural numbers N = {n : n is obtained by repeatedly adding 1 to itself};
• Prime numbers P = {p : p is an integer, and is not a product of two integers not equal to 0, 1, −1};
• Rational numbers Q = {m
n : m and n are integers};
• Open interval (a, b) = {x : x ∈R, a < x < b};
• Closed interval [a, b] = {x : x ∈R, a ≤x ≤b};
• Real polynomials R[t] = {p(t) = a0 + a1t + a1t2 + · · · + antn : ai are real numbers};
• Continuous functions C[0, 1] = {f : limx→a f(x) = f(a) for any 0 ≤a ≤1} on [0, 1];
• Unit sphere S2 = {(x1, x2, x3) : xi ∈R, x2
1 + x2
2 + x2
3 = 1} is contained in R3;
• X1 = {x : x ∈N, x ≤n};
• X2 = {x : x2 + 3x −10 = 0}.
Exercise 1.1 Present the following sets: the set Z of integers; the unit sphere Sn in Rn+1; the set GL(n) of
invertible n × n matrices; the set of latin alphabets in your name.
Lte X and Y be sets. We say X is a subset of Y if x ∈X implies x ∈Y . In this case, we
denote X ⊂Y (X is contained in Y ) or Y ⊃X (Y contains X). We clearly have
X ⊂Y,
Y ⊂Z
=⇒
X ⊂Z.
The power set P(X) (somtimes also denoted as 2X) of a set X is the collection of all subsets
of X.
The special notation ∅is reserved for the empty set, the set with no elements. The empty
set is a subset of any set.
3

Example 1.4 The following are all the subsets of the set {1, 2, 3}:
∅, {1}, {2}, {3}, {1, 2}, {1, 3}, {2, 3}, {1, 2, 3}.
Therefore the power set of {1, 2, 3} is
P{1, 2, 3} =
n
∅, {1}, {2}, {3}, {1, 2}, {1, 3}, {2, 3}, {1, 2, 3}
o
of all subsets of {1, 2, 3} is called the power set of {1, 2, 3}.
The construction of power sets demonstrates that sets themselves can become elements of some other sets
(which we usually call collection of sets). For example, the set
n
∅, {1, 2}, {1, 3}, {2, 3}
o
is the collection of subsets of {1, 2, 3} with even number of elements.
Exercise 1.2 How many elements are in the power set of {1, 2, · · · , n} (The answer suggests the reason for
denoting the power set by 2X)? How many of these contain even number of elements? How many contain odd
number?
Exercise 1.3 List all elements in P(P{1, 2}), the power set of the power set of {1, 2}.
Exercise 1.4 Given any real number ϵ > 0, ﬁnd a real number δ > 0, such that
{x : |x −1| < δ} ⊂{x : |x2 −1| < ϵ}.
Finally, we say X and Y are equal if X ⊂Y and X ⊃Y . In other words, in order to show
X = Y , we need to verify
• x ∈X implies x ∈Y ;
• y ∈Y implies y ∈X.
Exercise 1.5 Use the deﬁnition to show that the set X2 in Example 1.1 is equal to the set X2 in Example 1.3.
Exercise 1.6 We say X is a proper subset of Y if X ⊂Y and X ̸= Y . Show that if X is a proper subset of Y
and Y is a proper subset of Z, then X is a proper subset of Z.
Exercise 1.7 Can you ﬁnd two sets X and Y such that X is a subset of Y and Y is a subset of X?
1.2
Set Operations
The union of two sets X and Y is
X ∪Y = {x : x ∈X or x ∈Y }.
Note that in the union, we allow the possibility that x is in both X and Y . The intersection of
two sets is
X ∩Y = {x : x ∈X and x ∈Y }.
If X ∩Y = ∅, then there is no common elements shared by X and Y , and we say X and Y are
disjoint. Note the logical diﬀerence between the use of or in the union and the use of and in
the intersection.
The union and the intersection have the following properties:
4

• X ∩Y ⊂X ⊂X ∪Y ;
• Commutativity: Y ∪X = X ∪Y , Y ∩X = X ∩Y ;
• Associativity: (X ∪Y ) ∪Z = X ∪(Y ∪Z), (X ∩Y ) ∩Z = X ∩(Y ∩Z);
• Distibutivity: (X ∪Y ) ∩Z = (X ∩Z) ∪(Y ∩Z), (X ∩Y ) ∪Z = (X ∪Z) ∩(Y ∪Z);
• X ∪∅= X, X ∩∅= ∅.
The properties are not hard to prove. For example, the proof of the associativity of the union
involves the veriﬁcation of (X ∪Y ) ∪Z ⊂X ∪(Y ∪Z) and (X ∪Y ) ∪Z ⊃X ∪(Y ∪Z). The
ﬁrst inclusion is veriﬁed as follows
x ∈(X ∪Y ) ∪Z
⇒
x ∈X ∪Y or x ∈Z
⇒
x ∈X, or x ∈Y , or x ∈Z
⇒
x ∈X, or x ∈Y ∪Z
⇒
x ∈X ∪(Y ∪Z).
The second inclusion can be veriﬁed similarly.
The diﬀerence of two sets X and Y is
X −Y = {x : x ∈X and x ̸∈Y }.
We also call X −Y the complement of Y in X. The following are some useful properties about
the diﬀerence:
• X −Y = ∅⇔X ⊂Y , X −Y = X ⇔X ∩Y = ∅;
• deMorgan’s Law: X −(Y ∪Z) = (X −Y ) ∩(X −Z); X −(Y ∩Z) = (X −Y ) ∪(X −Z).
We note that deMorgan’s law says that the complement operation converts the union to the
intersection and vice versa.
..........................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
.............................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
...........................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
...........................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
....................................................................................................................................................................................
......................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................

Figure 1: operations on sets
The (cartesian) product of two sets X and Y is
X × Y = {(x, y) : x ∈X, y ∈Y }.
5

We also use Xn to denote the product of n copies of X.
When we mix several operations, we usually ﬁrst take product ×, then take union ∪or
intersection ∩, and ﬁnally take diﬀerence −. For example, x ∈(X −Y × Z) ∩W −U means
x ∈X, x ̸∈Y × Z, x ∈W, and x ̸∈U. The convention is similar to ﬁrst taking multiplication
and division, and then taking summation and subtraction in numerical computations.
Exercise 1.8 Prove the properties of the union, the intersection, and the diﬀerence.
Exercise 1.9 Find all the unions and intersections among A = {∅}, B = {∅, A}, C = {∅, A, B}.
Exercise 1.10 Express the following using sets X, Y , Z and operations ∪, ∩, −:
1. A = {x : x ∈X and (x ∈Y or x ∈Z)};
2. B = {x : (x ∈X and x ∈Y ) or x ∈Z};
3. C = {x : x ∈X, x ̸∈Y , and x ∈Z}.
Exercise 1.11 Determine which of the following statements are true:
1. X ⊂Z and Y ⊂Z ⇒X ∪Y ⊂Z;
2. X ⊂Z and Y ⊂Z ⇒X ∩Y ⊂Z;
3. X ⊂Z or Y ⊂Z ⇒X ∪Y ⊂Z;
4. Z ⊂X and Z ⊂Y ⇒Z ⊂X ∩Y ;
5. Z ⊂X and Z ⊂Y ⇒Z ⊂X ∪Y ;
6. Z ⊂X ∩Y ⇒Z ⊂X and Z ⊂Y ;
7. Z ⊂X ∪Y ⇒Z ⊂X and Z ⊂Y ;
8. X −(Y −Z) = (X −Y ) ∪Z;
9. (X −Y ) −Z = X −Y ∪Z;
10. X −(X −Z) = Z;
11. X ∩(Y −Z) = X ∩Y −X ∩Z;
12. X ∪(Y −Z) = X ∪Y −X ∪Z;
13. (X ∩Y ) ∪(X −Y ) = X;
14. X ⊂U and Y ⊂V ⇔X × Y ⊂U × V ;
15. X × (U ∪V ) = X × U ∪X × V ;
16. X × (U −V ) = X × U −X × V ;
17. (X −Y ) × (U −V ) = X × U −Y × V .
Exercise 1.12 Let A and B be subsets of X, prove that
A ⊂B ⇔X −A ⊃B ⇔A ∩(X −B) = ∅.
6

1.3
Maps
A map from a set X to a set Y is a process f that assigns, for each x ∈X, a unique y = f(x) ∈Y .
Sometimes, especially when X = Y , we also call f a transformation. In case Y is a set of
numbers, we also call f a function.
We call X and Y the domain and range of the map. We also call y the image (or the value)
of x. We may denote a map by the notation
f : X →Y,
x 7→f(x),
or the notation
f(x) = y : X →Y,
in which all the ingredients are indicated. We may omit some parts of the notation if the part
is clear from the context.
................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
.............................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
............................................................................................................................... ...........................
..................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
...................................................................................................................................................................................................................
...................................................................................................................................................................................................................
.................................................................................................................................................................................................
X
Y
domain
range
f
x
y
Figure 2: domain, range, and image
We emphasis that in order for a process f to be a map, two important properties have to
be established:
1. For any x ∈X, the process always goes through to the end and produces some y ∈Y ;
2. For any x ∈X, the end result y ∈Y of the process must be unique. In other words, the
process is single-valued.
It is especially important that whenever we deﬁne a new map f by certain process, we have
to subtantiate the process by showing that it always works (as in item 1) and the outcome is
unambiguous (as in item 2). Once substantiated, we say f is well-deﬁned.
Example 1.5 By the map f(x) = 2x2 −1 : R →R (equivalently, f : R →R, x 7→2x2 −1), we mean the
following process: For any x ∈R, we ﬁrst multiply x to itself to get x2, then we multiply 2 and subtract 1 to
get 2x2 −1. Since each step always works and gives unique outcome, the process is a map.
Example 1.6 The square root function f(x) = √x : [0, ∞) →R is the following process: For any x ≥0, we
ﬁnd a non-negative number y, such that multiplying y to itself yields x. Then f(x) = y. Again, since y always
exists and is unique, the process is a map.
Note that if we change [0, ∞) to R, then y does not exist for negative x ∈R, we cannot ﬁnd a number y
so that multiplying y to itself is −1. In other words, the ﬁrst condition for the process to be a map is violated,
and √x : R →R is not a map.
On the other hand, suppose we modify the process by no longer requiring y to be non-negative. Then for
any x ∈[0, ∞), the process always go through, except we will have two outcomes (one positive, one negative)
in general. Thus the second condition is violated and the process is not a map.
7

Example 1.7 The map Rθ(x1, x2) = (x1 cos θ −x2 sin θ, x2 sin θ + x1 cos θ) : R2 →R2 is the following process:
For any point (x1, x2) on the plane R2, we rotate (x1, x2) around the origin by angle θ in counterclockwise
direction.
Example 1.8 The map Age : Y4 →N is the process of subtracting the birth year from the current year.
Example 1.9 The map FA : Y2 →Y1, FA(red) = r, FA(green) = g, FA(blue) = b is the process of assigning
the ﬁrst alphabet of the name of the colour. The map A : Y2 →P(Y1), A(red) = {d, e, r}, A(green) = {e, g, n, r},
A(blue) = {b, e, l, u} is the process of assigning all the alphabets in the name of the colour.

h : X ∪Y →Z as follows
h(x) =

Exercise 1.14 For a subset A ⊂X, deﬁne the characteristic function
χA(x) =


if x ∈A

Prove the following equalities

For a map f : X →Y , the image of a subset A ⊂X is
f(A) = {f(a) : a ∈A} = {y : y = f(a)
for some a ∈A}.
In the other direction, the preimage of a subset B ⊂Y is
f −1(B) = {x : f(x) ∈B}.
In case B is a single point, we have the preimage
f −1(y) = {x : f(x) = y}
of a point y ∈Y .
Example 1.10 For the map f(x) = 2x2 −1 : R →R, both the domain and the range are R. The image
of the whole domain R is [−1, ∞). Moreover, the image of [0, ∞) is also [−1, ∞). The preimage of [0, ∞) is
(−∞, −1
√
2] ∪[ 1
√
2, ∞). The preimage of 1 is {1, −1}.
For the rotation map Rθ(x1, x2) = (x1 cos θ −x2 sin θ, x1 sin θ + x2 cos θ) : R2 →R2. Both the domain and
the range are R2. The image of the whole domain is also R2. Moreover, the image (and the preimage) of any
circle centered at the origin is the circle itself. If the circle is not centered at the origin, then the image (and
the preimage) is still a circle, but at a diﬀerent location.
For the ﬁrst alphabet map FA : Y2 →Y1, the domain is the three colours, the range is all the alphabets,
and the image is {b, g, r}. The preimage of {a, b, c} is {blue}. The preimage of {a, b, c, d, e} is still {blue}. The
preimage of {u, v, w, x, y, z} is ∅.

one-to-one

Figure 3: one-to-one and multiple-to-one
We say a map f : X →Y is one-to-one (or injective) if
f(x1) = f(x2)
=⇒
x1 = x2.
In other words, diﬀerent elements of X are mapped by f to diﬀerent elements of Y . We say f
is onto (or surjective) if
y ∈Y
=⇒
y = f(x)
for some x ∈X.
In other words, the image of the whole domain f(X) = Y . We say f is a one-to-one correspon-
dence (or bijective) if it is one-to-one and onto.
9

................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
.............................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
.............................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
.................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
........................................................................................................................................................................
........................................................................................................................................................................
........................................................................................................................
........................................................................................................................
onto
not onto
Figure 4: onto and not onto
Example 1.11 The map f(x) = 2x2 −1 : R →R is neither one-to-one nor onto. However, if we change the
range to [−1, ∞), then the map f(x) = 2x2 −1 : R →[−1, ∞) is onto (but still not one-to-one).
The square root function f(x) = √x : [0, ∞) →R is one-to-one but not onto. Again by changing the range
to [0, ∞), the function f(x) = √x : [0, ∞) →[0, ∞) becomes one-to-one and onto.
Example 1.12 The rotation map Rθ is a one-to-one correspondence.
The Age map is (almost certainly) not one-to-one and (deﬁnitely) not onto.
The ﬁrst alphabet map FA is ono-to-one but not onto. If we increase the number of colours in Y2, then
FA is getting more likely to be onto but still not one-to-one.
Exercise 1.20 Add one colour into Y2 so that FA is no longer one-to-one. Also add enough colours into Y2
so that FA becomes a one-to-one correspondence.

at most one point.

Figure 5: composition g ◦f
Given two maps f : X →Y and g : Y →Z (note that the range of f is the domain of g),
the composition g ◦f is deﬁned as follows
(g ◦f)(x) = g(f(x)) : X →Z.
10

A map f : X →Y is invertible if there is another map g : Y →X in the opposite direction,
such that
g ◦f = idX,
f ◦g = idY .
In other words,
g(f(x)) = x,
f(g(y)) = y.
We also call g the inverse of f.
The most important fact about the invertible map is the following.
Proposition 1.1 A map is invertible if and only if it is a one-to-one correspondence.
Proof: We only prove one-to-one correspondence implies invertible. The converse is left as
exercises (see Exercises 1.24 and 1.25).
Let f : X →Y be a one-to-one correspondence. Then we deﬁne g : Y →X to be the
following process: For any y ∈Y , we ﬁnd x ∈X, such that f(x) = y. Then we take g(y) = x.
We need to verify that such a process always goes through. The key is the existence of x.
Such existence is guranteed by the fact that f is onto.
We need to verify that the result of such a process is unique. The key is the uniqueness of
x. Such uniqueness is guranteed by the fact that f is one-to-one.
Thus we have established that g is a well-deﬁned map. It is then easy to verify that g is
indeed the inverse of f.
2
Example 1.13 Let A ⊂X be a subset. Then we have the natural inclusion map i(a) = a : A →X. For any
map f : X →Y , the composition f ◦i : A →Y is the restriction of f on A. The map is often denoted by f|A.

Example 1.15 The composition Rθ2 ◦Rθ1 means a rotation by angle θ1 followed by another rotation by angle

1.4
Equivalence Relation, Partition, and Quotient
In this section, we discuss the following three mutually equivalent concepts: equivalence rela-
tion, partition, and quotient.
We ﬁrst introduce the concepts on a set X. An equivalence relation on X is a collection of
pairs, denoted x ∼y for x, y ∈X, such that the following properties are satisﬁed:
• symmetry If x ∼y, then y ∼x;
• reﬂexivity For any x ∈X, we have x ∼x;
• transitivity If x ∼y and y ∼z, then x ∼z.
A partition of X is a decomposition of X into a disjoint union of nonempty subsets.
X =
[
i∈I
Xi,
Xi ̸= ∅,
Xi ∩Xj = ∅for i ̸= j.
(1)
A quotient of X is a surjective map q : X →I. Before explaining how the three concepts are
equivalent, let us ﬁrst look at some examples.
Example 1.16 For numbers x, y ∈R, deﬁne x ∼y if x and y have the same sign. This is an equivalence
relation. The corresponding partition is R = R−∪R0 ∪R+, with R−= (−∞, 0), R0 = {0}, and R+ = (0, ∞).
The corresponding quotient map is
q(x) =



+
if x > 0
0
if x = 0
−
if x < 0
:
R →{+, 0, −}.
On the other hand, deﬁne x ∼y if x ≤y. The relation is reﬂexive and transitive, but not symmetric.
Therefore this is not an equivalence relation.
Example 1.17 For integers x, y ∈Z, deﬁne x ∼y if x −y is even. This is an equivalence relation. The
corresponding partition is Z = Zeven ∪Zodd, where Zeven (Zodd) denotes the subset of all even (odd) numbers.
The corresponding quotient map is
q(x) =
 even
if x is even
odd
if x is odd
:
Z →{even, odd}.
Similarly, if we deﬁne x ∼y when x −y is a multiple of 3, then we also have an equivalence relation. The
corresponding partition is Z = Z0 ∪Z1 ∪Z2, where Zi is all the integers that have remainder i after devided by
3. The corresponding quotient is a surjective map onto I = {0, 1, 2}.
On othe other hand, if we deﬁne x ∼y when x −y is odd, then the relation is not an equivalence relation.
12

Example 1.18 On the product X × Y of two nonempty sets, deﬁne (x1, y1) ∼(x2, y2) if x1 = x2. This is an
equivalence relation. The corresponding partition is
X × Y =
[
x∈X
x × Y,
and the corresponding quotient is
q(x, y) = x : X × Y →Y.
Example 1.19 Let X be the all the people in the world. Consider the following relations:
1. descendant relation: x ∼y if x is a descendant of y;
2. blood relation: x ∼y if x and y have the same ancesters;
3. sibling relation: x ∼y if x and y have the same parents.
The descendant relation is neither reﬂexive nor symmetric, although it is transitive.
The blood relation is
reﬂexive and symmetric but not transitive. The sibling relation has all three properties and is an equivalence
relation.

Now let us ﬁnd out how the concepts of equivalence relations and partitions are related.
Given an equivalence relation ∼on a set X and an element x ∈X, the subset
[x] = {y : y ∼x}
of all elements related to x is the equivalence class determined by x. We also call x a represen-
tative of the equivalence class.
Proposition 1.2 Any two equivalence classes are either identical or disjoint. In fact, we have
[x] = [y] when x ∼y and [x] ∩[y] = ∅when x ̸∼y. Moreover, the whole set X is the union of
all equivalence classes.
Proof: Suppose x ∼y. Then for any z ∈[x], we have z ∼x by the deﬁnition of [x]. Then from
z ∼x, x ∼y, and the transitivity of ∼, we have z ∼y, i.e., z ∈[y]. This proves [x] ⊂[y]. On
the other hand, from x ∼y and the symmetry of ∼, we have y ∼x. Thus it can be similarly
proved that [y] ⊂[x], and we conclude that [x] = [y].
Now we want to show [x] ∩[y] = ∅when x ̸∼y. This is the same as proving [x] ∩[y] ̸= ∅
implies x ∼y. Thus we assume z ∈[x]∩[y]. Then we have z ∼x and z ∼y by the deﬁnition of
equivalence classes. By z ∼x and symmetry, we have x ∼z. By x ∼z, z ∼y, and transitivity,
we have x ∼y.
Finally, by the reﬂexivity, we have x ∈[x]. Therefore any element of X is in some equivalence
class. This implies that X is equal to the union of equivalence classes.
2
Proposition 1.2 tells us that any equivalence relation on X induces a partition into disjoint
union of equivalence classes. Conversely, given a partition (1), we may deﬁne a relation on X
as follows:
x ∼y
if x and y are in the same subset Xi.
It is easy to see that this is an equivalence relation, and the equivalence classes are given by
[x] = Xi
if x ∈Xi.
This establishes the one-to-one correspondence between equivalence relations on X and parti-
tions of X.
Example 1.20 Consider the same sign equivalence relation in Example 1.16. We have
[x] =

Example 1.21 Let V be a vector space. A straightline L in V passing through the origin 0 is a one-dimensional
subspace. For any two such straight lines L1 and L2, we have either L1 = L2 or L1 ∩L2 = 0. Therefore the
collection of all L −0 form a partition of V −0.
To see the equivalence relation coreesponding to the partition, we consider vectors u, v ∈V −0, they belong
to the same one-dimensional subspace if and only if one is a (nonzero) scalar multiple of the other: u = cv for
some c ∈R −0 (c ∈C −0 if V is a complex vector space).
The corresponding quotient is called the projective space.
Next we ﬁnd out the relation between the concepts of partitions and quotients. Given by a
partition (1), we may construct the set of subsets in the partition, which may be identiﬁed as
the index set for the subsets (∼= means “identify”):
X/∼= {[x] : x ∈X} = {Xi : i ∈I} ∼= I,
We call X/∼the quotient set of the partition (and of the corresponding equivalence relation).
Moreover, we have the canonical map
q : X →I,
q(x) = i
if x ∈Xi,
called the quotient map. Since all Xi are non-empty, the quotient map is surjective.
Concersely, given a surjective map q : X →I, we deﬁne Xi = q−1(i), the preimage of i ∈I.
Then it is not hard to see that this construction is the converse of the construction above. This
establishes the one-to-one correspondence between partitions of X and quotients of X.
We note that x, y ∈X are in the same Xi (i.e., x ∼y) if and only if q(x) = q(y). Besides
Examples 1.16 through 1.18, here are more examples on the relation between the three concepts.

The equivalence relation and the quotient are related by the following universal property.
Proposition 1.3 Let ∼be an equivalence relation on a set X, with the associated quotient
q : X →X/∼. Let f : X →Y be a map such that x1 ∼x2 implies f(x1) = f(x2). Then there
is a unique map ¯f : X/∼→Y , such that the following diagram is commutative
X

f ↘
↙¯f

i.e., ¯f ◦q = f.
Proof: The requirement ¯f ◦q = f means ¯f([x]) = f(x). We may take this as the deﬁnition of
¯f. All we need to show is that ¯f is well-deﬁned.
Suppose [x] = [y]. Then we have x ∼y. By the condition on f, we have f(x) = f(y).
Therefore we indeed have ¯f([x]) = ¯f([y]). This veriﬁes that the deﬁnition of ¯f is not ambiguous.

is essentially a function of the ﬁrst variable. Here we recall from Example 1.18 that the quotient set of X × Y
is X, and the quotient map X × Y →X is the projection to the ﬁrst coordinate.

Example 1.23 On R, deﬁne x ∼y if x −y is an integer. We verify that ∼is an equivalence relation. First,
since x −x = 0 is an integer, we have symmetry. Second, suppose x −y is an integer, then y −x = −(x −y) is
also an integer. This veriﬁes the reﬂexivity. Finally, if x−y and y −z are integers, then x−z = (x−y)+(y −z)
is also an integer. Therefore ∼is transitive.
Next, we identify the quotient set with the unit circle S1 = {eiθ : θ ∈R} in the complex plane, and identify
the quotient map with the exponential map E(x) = ei2πx : R →S1. First, if x = y + n for some integer n,
then we clearly have E(x) = ei2πyei2πn = ei2πy = E(y). Therefore by Proposition 1.3, we have a unique map
¯E : R/∼→S1, such that ¯E([x]) = ei2πx.
We claim that ¯E is invertible. By Proposition 1.1, it suﬃces to show ¯E is one-to-one and onto. Since every
point on S1 is of the form eiθ = ¯E(
 θ
2π

), we see ¯E is onto. On the other hand, if ¯E([x]) = ¯E([y]), then from
ei2πx = ei2πy we conclude that x−y is an integer. Therefore we have [x] = [y]. This proves that ¯E is one-to-one.
16

Since ¯E is invertible, we may use the map to identify the quotient set R/∼with the circle S1. Then from
[x] = [y] ⇔E(x) = E(y), we may conclude that E is identiﬁed with the quotient map.
Finally, we note that a map f : R →Y satisﬁes the condition of Proposition 1.3 if and only if f(x) = f(x+1),
i.e., f is a periodic map with period 1. By Proposition 1.3 and the discussion above, we conclude that periodic
maps with period 1 are in one-to-one correspondence with the maps ¯f : S1 →Y from the sphere.

2.1
Metric
Recall the deﬁnition of the continuity of a function f : R →R at a: For any ϵ > 0, there is
δ > 0, such that
|x −a| < δ
=⇒

(2)
Loosely speaking, this means
x is close to a

where the closeness is measured by the distance function d(u, v) = |u −v| between numbers.
Observing that the absolute values in (2) are distances, we can easily extend the notion
of continuity by considering appropriate distance functions. For example, by introducing the
distance
d((x1, · · · , xn), (a1, · · · , an)) =
q

Rn →R. For another example, we may deﬁne the distance
d(f(t), g(t)) = max0≤t≤1|f(t) −g(t)|

Deﬁnition 2.1 A metric space is a set X, together with a metric (also called distance)
d : X × X →R,
satisfying the following properties
• positivity d(x, y) ≥0, and d(x, y) = 0 ⇔x = y;
• symmetry d(x, y) = d(y, x);
• triangle inequality d(x, y) + d(y, z) ≥d(x, z).
Example 2.1 Over any set X, the function
d(x, y) =
 0
x = y
1
x ̸= y
is a metric, called discrete metric. Such a metric space X is a discrete space.
18

Example 2.2 Let X be all the subway stations in Hong Kong. For subway stations x, y ∈X, let d(x, y) be
the subway fare from station x to station y. Then d is a metric on X.
However, if X is changed to all the bus stops in Hong Kong and d(x, y) is the lowest bus fare (adding
fares for several bus rides if necessary) from a stop x to another stop y. Then d does not satisfy the symmetry
condition, although it clearly satisﬁes the other two conditions. Thus the lowest bus fare in Hong Kong is not
a metric.
On the other hand, the bus fares are calculated in diﬀerent ways in diﬀerent cities. In some other cities
(such as the cities where the bus fare is independent of the length of the ride), the lowest bus fare is indeed a
metric.

Example 2.5 For a ﬁxed prime number p, the p-adic metric on the set Q of rational numbers is deﬁned as
follows: Let x, y be diﬀerent rational numbers. Then we have the unique expression
x −y = m
n pa,
where m, n, a are integers, and neither m nor n is divisible by p. Deﬁne
dp-adic(x, y) =
1
p-factor of (x −y) = p−a.
In addition, we deﬁne dp-adic(x, x) = 0.
For example, for x = 7
6 and y = 3
4, we have x −y = 5
12 = 2−2 · 3−1 · 5. Therefore
2-factor of (x −y) is 2−2
=⇒
d2-adic(x, y) = 22 = 4,
3-factor of (x −y) is 3−1
=⇒
d3-adic(x, y) = 31 = 3,
5-factor of (x −y) is 51
=⇒
d5-adic(x, y) = 5−1 = 0.2,
7-factor of (x −y) is 70
=⇒
d7-adic(x, y) = 7−0 = 1.
We need to verify that the p-adic metric satisﬁes the three conditions. The only nontrivial veriﬁcation is
the triangle inequality. In fact, we will prove a stronger inequality
dp-adic(x, z) ≤max{dp-adic(x, y), dp-adic(y, z)}.
(7)
The triangle inequality follows from this (prove this!).
The inequality is clearly true when at least two of x, y, z are the same. Thus we assume x, y, z are distinct.
Let dp-adic(x, y) = p−a, dp-adic(y, z) = p−b. Then
x −y = m
n pa,
y −z = k
l pb,
(8)
and p does not divide any of k, l, m, n. Without loss of generality, we may assume a ≤b. Then
x −z = (x −y) + (y −z) = m
n pa + k
l pb = ml + nkpb−a
nl
pa.
Since a ≤b, the numerator of the fraction is an integer, which can contribute pc only for some non-negative c.
On the other hand, the denominator of the fraction cannot contribute any powers of p because neither n nor l
contains powers of p. As a result, the p-factor of x −z is pcpa = pc+a for some c ≥0. Then we conclude
dp-adic(x, z) = p−(c+a) ≤p−a = max{p−a, p−b},
where the last equality comes from the assumption a ≤b.

2.3
Open Subset
The system of balls in a metric space gives rise to the concept of open subsets.
Deﬁnition 2.2 A subset U of a metric space X is open if any point of U is contained in a ball
inside U.
In other words, for any a ∈U, there is an ϵ > 0, such that the ball B(a, ϵ) ⊂U.
................................................................................................................................................................................. ...........................
................................................
................................................
................................................
...............................................................................................................................................................................................
................................................
................................................
................................................
...............................................................................................................................................................................................
a
a ................................
U
U
a
Figure 10: deﬁnition of open subset
To get some feeling toward the concept, let us consider the two subsets of the plane R2 in
Figure 11. The subset A does not contain the boundary. Any point inside A has a ball around
it and is contained in A. The closer the point is to the boundary, the smaller the ball must
be. The important fact is that such a ball, as long as it is small enough, can always be found.
Therefore A is open. In contrast, B contains the boundary. At a boundary point, no matter
how small the ball is, it always contains points outside of B. So we cannot ﬁnd a ball around
the point and contained in B. Therefore B is not open.
Example 2.6 Let X be a discrete metric space. Then for any subset A ⊂X and a ∈A, we have B(a, 1) =
{a} ⊂A. Therefore any subset is open in the discrete space.

Example 2.7 We call (a, b) an open interval because it is indeed open in Rusual
1. In fact, for any x ∈(a, b),
we have ϵ = min{x −a, b −x} > 0 and B(x, ϵ) = (x −ϵ, x + ϵ) ⊂(a, b) (draw a picture to see the meaning of
this sentence).
The single point subset {a} ⊂Rusual is not open. However, according to the previous example, if we change
the metric from the usual one to the discrete one, then {a} is indeed open in Rdiscrete. This example indicates
that openness depends on the choice of the metrics.
The closed interval [0, 1] is not open in Rusual because no matter how small ϵ is, the ball B(0, ϵ) = (−ϵ, ϵ)
around 0 ∈[0, 1] is not contained in [0, 1]. Intuitively, the situation is similar to the subset B in Figure 11: 0 is
a “boundary point” of [0, 1].
Example 2.8 We may think of the “closed interval” [0, 1] as a subset of X = [0, 1] ∪[2, 3] (again equipped
with the usual metric). Note that the balls in X are
BX(a, ϵ) = {x ∈X : |x −a| < ϵ} = ([0, 1] ∪[2, 3]) ∩(a −ϵ, a + ϵ).
In particular, for any a ∈[0, 1] (including a = 0 and a = 1), we have BX(a, 1) ⊂[0, 1]. This shows that [0, 1] is
open in [0, 1] ∪[2, 3]. This example indicates that openness also depends on the choice of the ambient space.
Example 2.9 We claim that the subset U = {f ∈C[0, 1] : f(t) > 0 for all 0 ≤t ≤1} of all positive functions
is open in C[0, 1]L∞. To prove this, for any positive function f, we need to ﬁnd some ϵ > 0, such that any
function in the ball BL∞(f, ϵ) is positive.
Since [0, 1] is a bounded closed interval, f has positive lower bound ϵ > 0. Suppose h ∈BL∞(f, ϵ). Then
we have |h(t) −f(t)| < ϵ for all 0 ≤t ≤1. This implies h(t) −f(t) > −ϵ, so that
h(t) > f(t) −ϵ ≥0.
Thus we have shown that any function in BL∞(f, ϵ) is positive.
The following result provides lots of examples of open subsets.
Lemma 2.3 Any ball B(a, ϵ) is open.
Proof: We need to show that if x ∈B(a, ϵ), then we can ﬁnd small enough radius δ > 0, such
that B(x, δ) ⊂B(a, ϵ). From Figure 12, we see that any δ satisfying 0 < δ < ϵ −d(x, a) should
be suﬃcient for our purpose.
1To simplify presentation, we will often use subscripts to indicate the metric (and later on the topology) on
the set. Thus Rusual means R with the usual metric d(x, y) = |x −y|, and Rdiscrete means R with the discrete
metric.
24

Of course we need to rigorously prove our intuition. First of all, the assumption x ∈B(a, ϵ)
implies d(x, a) < ϵ. Therefore we can always ﬁnd δ satisfying 0 < δ < ϵ −d(x, a). Fix one such
δ, we then need to verify B(x, δ) ⊂B(a, ϵ), which means
d(y, x) < δ

This can be proved by using the triangle inequality,
d(y, a) ≤d(y, x) + d(x, a) < δ + d(x, a) < ϵ −d(x, a) + d(x, a) = ϵ.

The following theorem contains the most important properties of open subsets. The prop-
erties will become the axioms for the concept of topology.
Theorem 2.4 The open subsets of X satisfy the following properties:
1. ∅and X are open;
2. Any unions of open subsets are open;
3. Finite intersections of open subsets are open.
26

Note that inﬁnite intersections of open subsets may not be open. A counterexample is given
by ∩(−1
n, 1
n) = {0} in Rusual.
Proof of Theorem 2.4: The ﬁrst property is trivial.
Before proving the second property, we try to understand what we need to do. Let Ui be
open subsets of X (this is our assumption), i being some indices. The second property says
that the subset U = ∪Ui is also open. By the deﬁnition, this means that
x ∈U
=⇒
a ball B(x, ϵ) ⊂U,
(this should be our conclusion). The left picture in Figure 13 describes the situation.
Keeping in mind the assumption and the conclusion, we prove the second property.
x ∈U = ∪Ui
(assumption)
⇒
x ∈Ui some index i
(deﬁnition of union)
⇒
B(x, ϵ) ⊂Ui for some ϵ > 0
(deﬁnition of open)
⇒
B(x, ϵ) ⊂U.
(U contains Ui)
For the third property, we only need to consider the union of two open subsets. In other
words, we need to show that if U1 and U2 are open (assumption), then
x ∈U = U1 ∩U2
=⇒
a ball B(x, ϵ) ⊂U
(conclusion). The right picture in Figure 13 describes the situation.
x ∈U = U1 ∩U2
(assumption)
⇒
x ∈U1, x ∈U2
(deﬁnition of intersection)
⇒
B(x, ϵ1) ⊂U1, B(x, ϵ2) ⊂U2
(deﬁnition of open)
⇒
B(x, ϵ) ⊂B(x, ϵ1) ∩B(x, ϵ2) ⊂U1 ∩U2 = U.
The second ⇒is true for some choice of ϵ1, ϵ2 > 0. In the third ⇒, we take any ϵ satisfying
0 < ϵ ≤min{ϵ1, ϵ2}.

Figure 13: union and intersection of open subsets
As an application of the theorem, we know that in Rusual, any (possibly inﬁnite) union of
open intervals is open. It turns out that the converse is also true.
Theorem 2.5 A subset of Rusual is open ⇔It is a disjoint union of open intervals.
27

Proof: Let U ⊂R be an open subset. We will show that U is a disjoint union of open intervals.
The ﬁrst step is to construct the open intervals. For any x ∈U, we consider the collection
of open intervals (a, b) between x and U (i.e., satisfying x ∈(a, b) ⊂U). Since U is open, the
collection is not empty. Thus we may deﬁne
ax =
inf
x∈(a,b)⊂U a,
bx =
sup
x∈(a,b)⊂U
b.
Intuitively, (ax, bx) is the largest open interval between x and U, and should be one of the
intervals described in the theorem. We expect U to be the union of these intervals (for various
x ∈U).
Next we prove U = ∪x∈U(ax, bx).
By the construction of (ax, bx), we clearly have x ∈
(ax, bx) ⊂∪x∈U(ax, bx) for any x ∈U. This implies U ⊂∪x∈U(ax, bx). Conversely, if y ∈(ax, bx),
then either ax < y ≤x or x ≤y < bx. In case ax < y ≤x, by the deﬁnition of ax, we have an
interval (a, b), such that a < y ≤x and x ∈(a, b) ⊂U. This implies y ∈(a, b) ⊂U. By the
similar argument, we may prove that x ≤y < bx implies y ∈U. Combining the two cases, we
ﬁnd that (ax, bx) ⊂U. Therefore we also have ∪x∈U(ax, bx) ⊂U.
Having proved U = ∪x∈U(ax, bx), we turn to the disjointness. It is indeed possible for (ax, bx)
and (ay, by) to intersect for distinct x and y in U. However, we claim that if the intersection of
(ax, bx) and (ay, by) is not empty, then the two intervals must be identical.
Suppose z ∈(ax, bx) ∩(ay, by). Then z ∈U. From z ∈(ax, bx) ⊂U and the fact that
(az, bz) is the largest open interval between z and U, we have (ax, bx) ⊂(az, bz) ⊂U. Then
since (ax, bx) is the largest open interval between x and U, we conclude that (ax, bx) = (az, bz).
Similarly, (ay, by) = (az, bz). This proves that (ax, bx) and (ay, by) are idential.
Thus we see that in the union U = ∪x∈U(ax, bx), the open intervals on the right side are
either disjoint or identical. By eliminating the duplications on the right side, we may express
U as a disjoint union of open intervals.
2
Another application of Theorem 2.4 is the following useful technical result.
Lemma 2.6 A subset of a metric space is open if and only if it is a union of (open) balls.
Proof: It follows from Lemma 2.3 and the second part of Theorem 2.4 that a union of balls
is open. Conversely, suppose U is open, then for any x ∈U, we can ﬁnd ϵx > 0, such that
B(x, ϵx) ⊂U (the subscript x in ϵx is used to indicate the possible dependency of ϵ on x). After
constructing one such ball for each x ∈U, we have ∪x∈UB(x, ϵx) ⊂U. On the other hand,
U = ∪x∈U{x} ⊂∪x∈UB(x, ϵx). Therefore we have
U = ∪x∈UB(x, ϵx),
which expresses U as a union of balls.

2.4
Continuity
As promised before, the notion of continuity can be deﬁned for maps between metric spaces.
Deﬁnition 2.7 A map f : (X, dX) →(Y, dY ) between metric spaces is continuous if for any
a ∈X and ϵ > 0, there is δ > 0, such that
dX(x, a) < δ
=⇒
dY (f(x), f(a)) < ϵ.
(9)
Example 2.10 Let X be a discrete metric space and Y be any metric space. Then any map f : X →Y is
continuous. To see this, for any given a and ϵ, we simply take δ = 1. Because X is discrete, d(x, a) < 1 implies
x = a and d(f(x), f(a)) = 0 < ϵ.
Example 2.11 The evaluation map E(f) = f(0) : C[0, 1]L∞→Rusual is continuous. In fact, we have
d(E(f), E(g)) = |f(0) −g(0)| ≤max
0≤t≤0 |f(t) −g(t)| = dL∞(f, g).
This implies that (9) holds for E if we take δ = ϵ.
29

If we change L∞-metric to L1-metric, then E is no longer continuous. In order to see this, we take a in (9)
to be the constant zero function 0 and ϵ = 1. For any δ > 0, we will ﬁnd a function f, such that the left of (9)
holds:
dL∞(f, 0) =
Z 1
0
|f(t)|dt < δ,
but the right side fails:
d(E(f), E(0)) = |f(0)| ≥1.
The function can be easily constructed by considering its graph (draw the graph by yourself): We have a straight
line connecting f(0) = 1 to f(δ) = 0 and then f = 0 on [δ, 1]. Speciﬁcally, f is given by the following formula:
f(t) =

for 0 ≤t ≤δ

for δ ≤t ≤1 .
Example 2.12 Let f : X →Y and g : X →W be continuous maps. Then we consider h(x) = (f(x), g(x)) :
X →Y × W, where Y × W has the metric given in the ﬁrst part of Exercise 2.29. We prove that h is also
continuous.
For any given a ∈X and ϵ > 0, we can ﬁnd δ1 > 0 and δ2 > 0, such that dX(x, a) < δ1 implies
dY (f(x), f(a)) < ϵ and dX(x, a) < δ2 implies dW (g(x), g(a)) < ϵ.
Then dX(x, a) < min{δ1, δ2} implies
dY ×W (h(x), h(a)) = max{dY (f(x), f(a)), dW (g(x), g(a))} < ϵ. Thus by taking δ = min{δ1, δ2}, we established
(9) for h.
Example 2.13 We know from calculus that the summation, as a map from R2
usual to Rusual, is continuous.
A generalization of this fact is left as Exercise 2.38.
Now let us consider the summation Q2
p-adic →Qp-adic, (x, y) 7→x + y, where the p-adic metric on Q2 is
given in the ﬁrst part of Exercise 2.29. We claim that
dp-adic(x1 + x2, y1 + y2) ≤dp-adic((x1, x2), (y1, y2)) = max{dp-adic(x1, y1), dp-adic(x2, y2)}.
(10)
The inequality is similar to (7) and the proof is also similar. In fact, we let
x1 −x2 = m
n pa, dp-adic(x1, x2) = p−a;
y1 −y2 = k
l pb, dp-adic(y1, y2) = p−b,
which is similar to (8). Then by the similar argument as in Example 2.5, we have c ≥0, such that
dp-adic(x1 + x2, y1 + y2) = p−(c+min{a,b}) ≤max{p−a, p−b}.
The ineqiality (10) implies that (9) is true for our summation map if we take δ = ϵ.

Theorem 2.8 The following are equivalent for a map f : X →Y between metric spaces:
1. The map f is continuous;
31

2. The preimage f −1(U) of any open subset U ⊂Y is in X;
3. The preimage f −1(B(y, ϵ)) of any ball in Y is open in X.
Proof: Suppose f : X →Y is continuous. Let U ⊂Y be an open subset. The following shows
that f −1(U) is open,
a ∈f −1(U)
(assumption)
⇒
f(a) ∈U
(deﬁnition)
⇒
B(f(a), ϵ) ⊂U
for some ϵ > 0
(U is open)
⇒
B(a, δ) ⊂f −1(B(f(a), ϵ)) ⊂f −1(U) for some δ > 0.
(continuity (12))
This completes the proof that the ﬁrst property implies the second.
As a consequence of Lemma 2.3, the second property implies the third.
Finally, assume f has the third property. We need to show that f is continuous. For any
a ∈X and ϵ > 0, we take y = f(a) in the third property and ﬁnd that f −1(B(f(a), ϵ)) is open.
Since a ∈f −1(B(f(a), ϵ)), we have some δ > 0 such that (12) holds. This proves the continuity
of f.
2
Corollary 2.9 The compositions of continuous maps are continuous.
Proof: Suppose f : X →Y and g : Y →Z are continuous. Let U ⊂Z be open. We need to
show (gf)−1(U) = f −1(g−1(U)) is open. The continuity of g implies that g−1(U) is open. The
continuity of f then further implies that f −1(g−1(U)) is open.
2
Example 2.14 Consider the summation map σ(x, y) = x + y : R2
Euclidean →Rusual. To check the continuity
of σ, we only need to consider the preimage of balls in R, which are simply open intervals. Note that σ−1(a, b)
is an open strip in R2 at 135 degrees. It is intuitively clear (and not diﬃcult to show) that σ−1(a, b) is indeed
open. Therefore σ is continuous.

Example 2.16 The set M(n, n) of all n × n matrices may be identiﬁed with Rn2 in an obvious way. With the

Therefore det−1(R −{0}) is an open subset of M(n, n). This open subset is simply all the invertible matrices.

2.5
Limit Point
Deﬁnition 2.10 x ∈X is a limit point of A ⊂X if for any ϵ > 0, there is a point a, such that
1. a ∈A,
2. a ̸= x,
3. d(a, x) < ϵ.
The collection of limit points of A is denoted A′.
In other words, there are points of A that are arbitrarily close but not equal to x. Equiva-
lently, (A −x) ∩B(x, ϵ) ̸= ∅for any ϵ > 0. For the relation between this deﬁnition and the one
in terms of convergent sequences that you probably learned in calculus, see Exercise 2.56.

Example 2.18 Consider the following subsets of Rusual: A1 = [0, 1] ∪{2}, A2 =

1, 1
2, 1
3, · · ·

, A3 = Q,
A4 = Z. The set of limit points are respectively A′
1 = [0, 1], A′
2 = {0}, A′
3 = R, A′
4 = ∅.
2 is not a limit point of A1 because a ∈A1 and d(a, 2) < 0.5 ⇒a = 2. Therefore the three conditions
cannot be satisﬁed at the same time. Intuitively, the reason for 2 not to be a limit point is because it is an
isolated point of A, i.e., there are no other points of A nearby it. The same intuition applies to A4, in which all
the points are isolated, so that no points are limit points.
Example 2.19 In Rn
Euclidean, the closed ball ¯B(a, ϵ) = {x ∈Rn : d(x, a) ≤ϵ} is the set of limit points of the
ball B(a, ϵ).
Example 2.20 A foundamental theorem in approximation theory is that any continuous function on [0, 1] may
be uniformly approximated by polynomials. Speciﬁcally, this says that for any continuous function f(t) and
ϵ > 0, there is a polynomial p(t), such that |f(t) −p(t)| < ϵ for all 0 ≤t ≤1. It follows from this that the limit
points of the subset R[t] ⊂C[0, 1]L∞of polynomials is the whole space C[0, 1].
Example 2.21 Consider the subset A = {f : f(1) = 1} of C[0, 1]. We would like to ﬁnd out whether the
constant zero function 0 is a limit point of A.
Suppose we equip C[0, 1] with the L∞-metric.
Then the three conditions in the deﬁnition means the
following for a function f (taken as a in the deﬁnition): f(1) = 1, f ̸= 0, |f(t)| < ϵ for all 0 ≤t ≤1. The
conditions are contradictory for ϵ = 1. Therefore 0 is not a limit point of A in the L∞-metric.
Now we take L1-metric instead. The conditions become f(1) = 1, f ̸= 0,
Z 1
0
|f(t)|dt < ϵ. No matter
how small ϵ is, we can always ﬁnd a big N (N > ϵ−1 is good enough), such that f(t) = tN satisﬁes the three
conditions. Therefore 0 is a limit point of A in the L1-metric.
Example 2.22 By taking ϵ = 1, we see that any subset of a discrete metric space has no limit points. In
particular, the set of limit points of B(a, 1) = {a} is ∅, which is diﬀerent from the “closed ball” ¯B(a, 1) =whole
space. This is in contrast with the balls in a Euclidean space (see Example 2.19).

2.6
Closed Subset
The notion of closed subsets is motivated by the intuition that nothing “escapes” from them.
Deﬁnition 2.11 C ⊂X is closed if all limit points of C are inside C.
Our examples on limit points gives us many examples (and counterexamples) of closed
subsets.
In Example 2.18, only A′
1 and A′
4 are contained respectively in A1 and A4. Therefore A1
and A4 are closed, while A2 and A3 are not closed. From Example 2.19, we see that points of
distance ϵ from a are limit points of the Euclidean ball B(a, ϵ). Since such points are not in
the Euclidean ball, B(a, ϵ) is not closed. From Example 2.20, the subset R[t] of polynomials
is not closed in C[0, 1]L∞. In fact, it is also not closed in C[0, 1]L1. By 2.22, any subset of a
discrete metric space is closed. Finally, by Exercise 2.57, any ﬁnite subset of a metric space has
no limit points and is therefore closed.
Exercise 2.63 The following are the four possibilities for a subset A of a metric space X.
1. A is open and closed;
2. A is open but not closed;
3. A is closed but not open;
4. A is neither open nor closed.
For each possibility, construct an example of X and A, with A ̸= ∅, X. Moreover, prove that the only open and
closed subsets of X = Rusual are ∅and R.
Exercise 2.64 Continuing Exercise 2.60, suppose A is a closed subset of a metric space X. Is A still closed if
we modify the metric as in Exercise 2.47?
Exercise 2.65 Prove that in an ultrametric space (see Exercise 2.19), any ball is also closed.
35

Now we discuss major properties of closed subsets.
Theorem 2.12 C ⊂X is closed if and only if X −C is open.
Proof:
X −C is open
⇔
x ∈X −C implies B(x, ϵ) ⊂X −C for some ϵ > 0
⇔
x ̸∈C implies C ∩B(x, ϵ) = ∅for some ϵ > 0
⇔
x ̸∈C implies (C −x) ∩B(x, ϵ) = ∅for some ϵ > 0
⇔
x ̸∈C implies x is not a limit point of C
⇔
x is a limit point of C implies x is in C
⇔
C is closed.
The third equivalence comes from C = C −x for x ̸∈C.
2
As an application, we know from Example 2.6 that any subset of a discrtete space is open.
By the theorem, any subset of a discrete space is also closed.
By making use of f −1(Y −C) = X −f −1(C), Theorem 2.12 implies the following.
Corollary 2.13 A map between metric spaces is continuous if and only if the preimage of any
closed subset is closed.
By making use of deMorgan’s law X −∪Ai = ∩(X −Ai) and X −∩Ai = ∪(X −Ai), Lemma
2.4 and Theorem 2.12 imply the following.
Corollary 2.14 The closed subsets of a metric space X satisfy the following properties:
1. ∅and X are closed;
2. Any intersections of closed subsets are closed;
3. Finite unions of closed subsets are closed.
Example 2.23 The solutions of the equation x2 −y2 = 1 is a pair of hyperbola in R2. From the picture it is
easy to see that the solution set H is a closed subset of R2. Here is a rigorous way of proving the closedness
of H: Consider the map f(x, y) = x2 −y2 : R2 →R, both sides with the usual metric. Since the map is
continuous and {1} ⊂R is a closed subset, we see that f −1(1) is a closed subset. But f −1(1) is nothing but H.
The argument generalizes to higher dimensional cases, when it is hard to argue directly from the picture
that some solution set is closed. For example, the set of 2 × 2 singular matrices is a closed subset of R4 because
d(x, y, z, w) = det
 x
y
z
w

= xw −yz : R4 →R is continuous and {0} ⊂R is closed.

3
Graph and Network
3.1
Seven Bridges in K¨onigsberg
Perhaps the ﬁrst work which deserves to be considered as the beginnings of topology is due to
Leonhard Euler2. In 1736 Euler published a paper entitled “Solutio problematis ad geometriam
situs pertinentis” which translates into English as “The solution of a problem relating to the
geometry of position”. The title itself indicates that Euler was aware that he was dealing with
a diﬀerent type of geometry where distance was not relevant.
The problem studied in that paper was concerned with the geography of the city of K¨onigsberg.
The city was divided by a river. In the river there were two islands. Seven bridges connected
the two parts of the city and the two islands together.

Euler’s questions is: Is it possible to cross the seven bridges in a single journey (i.e., cross
each bridge exactly once)? The problem may be abstracted into Figure 19, in which each region
of the city becomes a point and each bridge becomes a line connecting two points.
.................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................

Deﬁnition 3.1 A graph consists of ﬁnitely many points (called vertices), and ﬁnitely many
lines (called edges) connecting these points. We have the following concepts about a graph:
1. A path is a sequence of vertices V1, V2, · · · , Vk, and a sequence of edges E1, E2, · · · , Ek−1,
such that Ei connects Vi and Vi+1. We say the path connects the vertices V1 and Vk;
2Born April 15, 1707 in Basel, Switzerland; Died September 18, 1783 in St. Petersburg, Russia
38

2. A cycle is a path such that V1 = Vk;
3. A graph is connected if any two vertices can be connected by a path;
4. The degree of a vertex is the number of edges emanating from it.

Figure 20: a path in a graph
The K¨onigsberg graph is connected. The degrees of A, B, D are 3. The degree of C is 5.
Euler’s problem can be generalized to the following: Given a graph, is it possible to ﬁnd a
path, so that each edge appears exactly once in the path?
Theorem 3.2 Let G be a connected graph. Then the following are equivalent:
1. There is a path in which each edge appears exactly once;
2. There are at most two vertices with odd degree.
We will need the following preparatory result (Theorem 3.3) to prove the theorem above.
In the remaining part of this section, we will discuss this result and its applications. The proof
of Theorem 3.2 will be deferred to the next section.
Theorem 3.3 In any graph we have
X
all vertices V
deg(V ) = 2(number of edges).
In case of the K¨onigsberg graph, we have
deg(A) + deg(B) + deg(C) + deg(D) = 3 + 3 + 3 + 5 = 2 × 7.
We analyse more closely why we have this equality for the K¨onigsberg graph. In deg(A), we
are counting the edges labeled 1, 2, and 3. In deg(C), we are counting the edges labeled 1, 2,
4, 5, and 6. Thus the edge labeled 3 appears once in deg(A) and once in deg(C). Moreover,
it does not appear in the other two degrees. In fact, because each edge has exactly two end
vertices, each of the seven edges appear in exactly two degrees in the summation. This is why
we have twice of seven on the right side of the equation.
The discussion on the K¨onigsberg graph can be generalized to any graph. Again, the key
property of the graph is that each edge has exactly two end vertices. Therefore each graph
appears exactly twice in the summation
P
all vertices V deg(V ). Consequently, the summation is
twice of the number of vertices.
39

another loop
Figure 21: loops
If we are little bit more careful, we need to worry about a special case. A loop is an edge
such that the two ends are the same vertex. Suppose a loop is attached to a vertex V . Then
the loop is emanating from V in two ways. Therefore in deg(V ), this loop is counted twice.
This is balanced by the right side of the formula.

about connected graph?

Figure 22: possible developments of “sprouts” starting from two points
Next is an interesting application of the degree formula in Theorem 3.3. The game “sprouts”
starts with a number of dots on a sheet of paper. Several players take turns to draw a curve and
add a new dot anywhere along the new curve. The curve must satisfy the following conditions
1. starts and ends at dots (possibly the same);
2. cannot cross itself;
3. cannot cross any previous curves;
4. cannot pass any other dot.
40

Another rule of the game is
5. there cannot be more than three curves emanating from any dot.
The ﬁrst player who cannot add more curve loses.
Theorem 3.4 Starting with n dots, it takes at most 3n steps to ﬁnish the game.
Proof: The game creates a graph, with dots as the vertices, and (half) curves as edges. Each
step creates one more vertex, and two more edges. Therefore after N steps, the number of
vertices is v = n + N, and the number of edges is e = 2N.
By the ﬁfth rule, the degree of each vertex is ≤3. Therefore by Theorem 3.3, we have
inequality 2e ≤3v. Putting v = n+N and e = 2N into the inequality, we obtain 4N ≤3(n+N).
This is the same as N ≤3n.
2
As a matter of fact, the game has at most 3n −1 steps.
Exercise 3.2 What would be the outcome of the game if we make any one of the following changes to the rule:
1. The ﬁfth rule is changed from three curves to four curves;
2. Two dots are added in each step instead of just one dot;
3. The game is carried out over a torus or a M¨obius band instead of the plane.
3.2
Proof of One-Trip Criteria
In this section, we prove Theorem 3.2.
Proof of 1 ⇒2: Let α be a path satisfying 1. Suppose α connects a vertex V to a vertex V ′.
We consider the vertices passed when we traverse along α.
Whenever α passes a vertex W diﬀerent from V and V ′, α must arrive W along an edge and
leave W along another edge. This pair of edges will not be passed again, by the assumption on
α. Moreover, each edge emanating from W is passed sooner or later. Therefore
deg(W) = 2(number of times α passes W),
so that the degree of any vertex other than V and V ′ is even.

41

The counting is the same at V , except the starting edge along which α leaves V . If α does
not end at V (i.e., V ′ ̸= V ), then this extra edge gives us
deg(V ) = 1 + 2(number of times α comes back to V after starting from V ).
Similarly, in case V ′ ̸= V we have
deg(V ′) = 2(number of times α reaches V ′ before ﬁnally ﬁnishing at V ′) + 1.
Therefore V and V ′ are the only two vertices with odd degrees.
If V = V ′, we have
deg(V ) = 2(number of times α passes V ) + 2,
where 2 counts the edges along which α starts from and ends at V . Therefore all vertices have
even degree.
2
We note that in case we do have a pair of vertices with odd degrees, the proof above shows
that the pair must be the two end vertices of the path α. If we do not have vertices of odd
degrees, then the path must be a cycle.
Proof of 2 ⇒1: Inspired by the discussion above, we will actually try to prove the following:
1. If all the vertices have even degrees, then starting from any vertex, we are able to ﬁnd a
cycle in which each edge appears exactly once;
2. If two vertices V and V ′ have odd degrees, and all the other vertices have even degrees,
then we are able to ﬁnd a path beginning from V and ending at V ′, and in which each
edge appears exactly once.
We prove these statements by induction on the number of edges. If the graph has only
one edge, then the statements are obviously true. Now suppose the statements are true for all
(connected) graphs with number of edges < n, n ≥2. Then we consider a connected graph G
with the number of edges = n.
Suppose all vertices of G have even degrees. We need to prove the ﬁrst statement by starting
from an arbitrary vertex V . We pick an edge E emanating from V . We denote by V ′ the other
vertex of E. Then we remove E and get a graph G′ with n −1 edges. Now we need to consider
two cases.
Case 1: If E is loop, i.e., V = V ′, then degG′(V ) = degG(V ) −2 is even. Moreover, for any
other vertex W, degG′(W) = degG(W) remains even. The graph G′ is clearly still connected.
By applying the ﬁrst statement in the inductive hypothesis to G′, we can ﬁnd a cycle α′ of G′
beginning and ending at V , such that each edge of G′ appears exactly once in the path. Let
α be the path that begins at V , follows α′ to arrive back at V , and then follows E back to V
again. Then α is a cycle in which each edge of G appears exactly once.
Case 2: If E is not a loop, i.e., V ̸= V ′, then degG′(V ) = degG(V ) −1, degG′(V ′) =
degG(V ′) −1 are odd. Moreover, for any other vertex W, degG′(W) = degG(W) remains even.
42

We claim that G′ has to be connected. If not, then G′ is split into two disjoint parts G′
1 and
G′
2, such that V is a vertex of G′
1 and V ′ is a vertex of G′
2 (since G is connected, the break
oﬀmust happen at E). In particular, G′
1 and G′
2 are two graphs with exactly one odd degree
vertex each. But it is a consequence of Theorem 3.3 that this cannot happen (see Exercise 3.1).
Thus we conclude that G′ has to be connected.
Now we apply the second statement in the inductive hypothesis to the connected graph G′,
which has V ans V ′ as the only odd degree vertices. we can ﬁnd a path α′ of G′ connecting V
to V ′, such that each edge of G′ appears exactly once in α′. Now let α be the path that begins
at V , follows α′ to arrive at V ′, and then follows E to V . Then α is a cycle in which each edge
appears exactly once.
In case all except two vertices have even degrees, we need to take E to be the edge emanating
from an odd degree point. Then we have to consider the possibility that removing E may break
G into two parts. The details is a little bit more complicated. But the key idea is similar. We
omit the proof here.

3.3
Euler Formula
The next step in freeing mathematics from being a subject about measurement was also due
to Euler. In 1750 he wrote a letter to Christian Goldbach3 which gives Euler’s famous formula
for a connected graph on a 2-dimensional sphere
v −e + f = 2
3Born March 18, 1690 in K¨onigsberg, Prussia (now Kaliningrad, Russia); Died November 20, 1764 in Moscow,
Russia. Goldbach is best remembered for his conjecture, made in 1742 in a letter to Euler, that every even
integer greater than 2 is the sum of two primes. The conjecture is still open.
43

where v is the number of vertices, e is the number of edges and f is the number of faces (regions
of the sphere divided by the graph).
It is interesting to realize that this, really rather simple, formula seems to have been missed
by Archimedes and Descartes although both wrote extensively on polyhedra. Again the reason
must be that to everyone before Euler, it had been impossible to think of geometrical properties
without measurement being involved.

Figure 25: numbers of vertices, edges, and faces
If we punch a hole on the sphere, then we get a plane. Correspondingly, any graph on the
sphere and not touching the hole becomes a graph on the plane. This graph enclose several
ﬁnite regions and one inﬁnite region in the plane. For a graph in the plane, usually we only
count the number of ﬁnite regions. This number f is one less than the corresponding number
for the graph in the sphere. Therefore for planar graphs the Euler formula is the following.

Theorem 3.5 For any connected planar graph, we have
v −e + f = 1.
Proof: We prove by induction on the number of edges of a graph. If there is only one edge,
then we have two possibilities: (i) The edge has two distinct end vertices. Then v = 2, e = 1,
f = 0. (ii) The edge is a loop. Then v = 1, e = 1, f = 1. In either case, we have v −e + f = 1.
Assume the theorem is true for graphs with e < n. Let G be a connected graph in plane
with e = n.
If f ̸= 0, then there is at least one ﬁnite region. Let E be an edge on the boundary of
one ﬁnite region. If we remove E, then the new graph G′ is still connected. Since E is on the
boundary of one ﬁnite region, we get one less region after removing E. Of course we also get
one less edge and the same number of vertices after removing E. Therefore f(G′) = f(G) −1,
e(G′) = e(G) −1, v(G′) = v(G). Since e(G′) = n −1 < n, by inductive assumption we have
v(G′) −e(G′) + f(G′) = 1. Therefore v(G) −e(G) + f(G) = 1.
44

Figure 27: proof of Euler formula in case f ̸= 0
If f = 0, then there is no ﬁnite region. We claim that there must be a vertex with degree
1. We prove the claim by contradiction. Assume all the vertices have degrees ≥2. Then we
start from some vertex and going around the graph along the edges. Because any vertex has at
least two edges emanating from it, we can choose a path without “backtracking”: going from
one end of an edge to the other end and then coming back. Because there are ﬁnitely many
vertices, sooner or later the path must cross itself at some vertex. In this way, we are able to
ﬁnd a cycle, which must enclose a ﬁnite region. This ﬁnite region may be further divided by
the graph. In any case, we should have f > 0, a contradiction.

Thus we have established the existence of vertices of degree 1. Let V be one such vertex,

still connected. Moreover, we have f(G′) = f(G), e(G′) = e(G) −1, v(G′) = v(G) −1. Since
e(G′) = n −1 < n, by inductive assumption we have v(G′) −e(G′) + f(G′) = 1. Therefore
v(G) −e(G) + f(G) = 1.

Embedding Graphs
In this section, we discuss an interesting application of Euler formula.
Consider three houses and three wells in Figure 29. Can you construct paths from each
house to each well without having any two paths crossing each other? Think of the houses and
wells as vertices and the paths connecting them as edges. Then the situation may be abstracted
to the left graph K3,3 in Figure 30.
The answer to the question is no, according to the following famous theorem due to Kaz-
imierz Kuratowski4.

Theorem 3.6 A graph is planar if and only if it does not contain any of the graphs K3,3 and
K5 in Figure 30.

Figure 30: basic non-planar graphs
The proof of the theorem is rather complicated. Here we only show that the two graphs are
not planar.
(Partial) Proof: Consider the graph K5 ﬁrst. Suppose it is planar. Then by the Euler formula,
v −e + f = 1 and e = 10, v = 5, we have f = 6. Now the boundary of each face consists of
at least three edges (because the boundary is a cycle). Moreover, any “internal edge” is on the
boundary of exactly two faces, and any “boundary edge” is on the boundary of only one face.
Therefore if we let ei and eb be the numbers of internal and boundary edges, then
ei + eb = e = 10,
2ei + eb ≥3f = 18.
From 2(10 −eb) + eb ≥18 we see that eb ≤2. However, this would imply that the graph
looks like either one in Figure 31. In case eb = 2, there are two edges joining the same pair of
vertices. In case eb = 1, there is a loop. Neither situation can be found inside the two graphs.
The contradiction proves that K5 cannot be planar.

............................................................

eb = 1
a graph
a graph
Figure 31: what K5 would be like if it is embedded in plane
46

For the graph K3,3, we have e = 9, v = 6, and from v −e + f = 1, f = 4. The similar
argument would lead to ei + eb = 9, 2ei + eb ≥3f = 12, so that eb ≤6, which would not lead to
contradiction as before. Thus one more input is needed. Observe that any cycle in the graph
should consist of at least 4 edges, and the boundary of any face must be a cycle. Therefore any
face has at least 4 edges on its boundary. This shows that the inequality may be improved to
2ei + eb ≥4f = 16. Then we are able to conclude eb ≤2, and the same contradiction arises.

3.5
Platonic Solids
Plato5 founded, on land which had belonged to Academos, a school of learning called the
Academy. The institution was devoted to research and instruction in philosophy and sciences.
Over the door of the Academy was written: Let no one unversed in geometry enter here.
The ﬁve Platonic solids are given in Figure 32. They are characterized by the extreme
symmetry in numbers: All the faces have the same number of edges on the boundary; All the
vertices have the same number of edges emanating from them (i.e., same degree).

Exercise 3.8 What is the condition on positive integers v, e, f that can be realized by a connected graph

Exercise 3.9 Suppose we have a connected graph embeded in a sphere such that any face has exactly three
edges. Prove that e = 3v −6, f = 2v −4.
Hint: Prove 3f = 2e and then combine this with the Euler formula.

4
Topology
4.1
Topological Basis and Subbasis
The key topological concepts and theories for metric spaces can be introduced from balls. In
fact, if we carefully examine the deﬁnitions and theorems about open subsets, closed subsets,
continuity, etc., then we see that we have used exactly two key properties about the balls.
Whether or not we have a metric, as long as we have a system of balls satisfying these two
properties, we should be able to develop similar topological theory. This observation leads to
the concept of topological basis.
Deﬁnition 4.1 A topological basis on a set X is a collection B of subsets of X, such that
1. x ∈X ⇒x ∈B for some B ∈B;
2. x ∈B1 ∩B2, with B1, B2 ∈B ⇒x ∈B ⊂B1 ∩B2 for some B ∈B.
............................................................................. ...........................
............................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
x
B1
B2
............................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
x B
B1
B2
.........................................
Figure 33: deﬁnition of topological basis
We may think of subsets in B as “generalized balls”. These subsets are not necessarily pro-
duced from a distance function. The following lemma is a useful and simple way of recognizing
topological basis (the converse is however not true).
Lemma 4.2 Suppose a collection B satisﬁes the ﬁrst condition and
B1, B2 ∈B
=⇒
B1 ∩B2 ∈B
or
B1 ∩B2 = ∅.
Then B is a topological basis.
Given the property in the lemma, the second condition for topological basis is met by taking
B = B1 ∩B2.
Example 4.1 The following collections are all topological bases on R because they satisfy the condition of
Lemma 4.2.
B1
=
{(a, b) : a < b}
B2
=
{[a, b) : a < b}
B3
=
{(a, b] : a < b}
B4
=
B1 ∪B2
B5
=
{(a, +∞) : all a}
B6
=
{(−∞, a) : all a}
B7
=
{(a, b) : a < b, and a, b are rational}
B8
=
{[a, b) : a < b, and a, b are rational}
B9
=
{R −F : F is ﬁnite}
B10
=
{[a, b] : a ≤b}
49

On the other hand, B2 ∪B3, B = {open intervals of length 1}, and B′ = {[a, b] : a < b} are not topological bases.
To see B2 ∪B3 is not a topological basis, let us consider 0 ∈(−1, 0]∩[0, 1). If B satisﬁes 0 ∈B ⊂(−1, 0]∩[0, 1),
then we necessarily have B = {0}, which is not in B2 ∪B3. Therefore the second condition for the topological
basis is not satisﬁed.
Example 4.2 The following collections are all topological bases on R2.
B1
=
{(a, b) × (c, d) : a < b, c < d}
B2
=
{open disks}
B3

{open triangles}
B4
=
{[a, b) × [c, d) : a < b, c < d}

{[a, b) × (c, d) : a < b, c < d}
B6
=
{(a, b) × (c, d) : a < b, c < d, and a, b, c, d are rational}
We may apply Lemma 4.2 to show that B1, B4, B5, B6 are topological bases. Although B2 and B3 do not satisfy
the condition of Lemma 4.2, they are still topological bases (see Figure 34).

Exercise 4.1 For any set X, prove that the collections B1 = {X −F : F ⊂X is ﬁnite} and B2 = {X −C :

Exercise 4.2 Prove the balls in a metric space form a topological basis. More precisely, prove the following:
Suppose d(x, x1) < ϵ1 and d(x, x2) < ϵ2. Then there is ϵ > 0, such that d(x, y) < ϵ implies d(y, x1) < ϵ1 and
d(y, x2) < ϵ2.
Exercise 4.3 Let B be a topological basis on X, and f : Y →X be a map. Is f −1(B) = {f −1(B) : B ∈B} a
topological basis on Y ?
Exercise 4.4 Let B be a topological basis on X, and f : X →Y be a map. Is f(B) = {f(B) : B ∈B} a
topological basis on Y ?
Exercise 4.5 Let BX and BY be a topological basis on X. Is BX × BY = {B × C : B ∈BX, C ∈BY } a
topological basis on X × Y ?
Exercise 4.6 Suppose B and B′ are topological bases on X. Which of the following are also topological bases?
1. B1 = {B ∈B or B′};
2. B2 = {B ∈B and B′};
3. B3 = {B ∈B and ̸∈B′};
4. B4 = {B ∩B′ : B ∈B and B′ ∈B′};
5. B5 = {B ∪B′ : B ∈B and B′ ∈B′}.
For any collection S of subsets of X, the collection
B = {S1 ∩· · · ∩Sn :
Si ∈S, n ≥1} ∪{X}
is, by Lemma 4.2, a topological basis.
We call S the topological subbasis that induces (or
generates) B. Note that we add X to the collection for the sole purpose of making sure the
ﬁrst condition for topological bases is satisﬁed. If S satisﬁes ∪S∈SS = X, then we do not need
to include X.
Example 4.5 Let S = {open intervals of length 1} be as in Example 4.1. Then S is a topological subbasis
on R. The topological basis induced by S is B = {open intervals of length ≤1}. Note that we do not need to
include the whole space R here.
Another example of topological subbasis on R is given by S = {(a, ∞) : all a} ∪{(−∞, a) : all a}. Since
(a, b) = (−∞, b) ∩(a, ∞), it induces the subbasis B1 in Example 4.1.
Example 4.6 On any set X, the topological subbasis S = {X −{x} : all x ∈X} induces the topological basis
B = {X −F : F ⊂X is ﬁnite}.
Example 4.7 In Example 4.4, we constructed a topological basis on C[0, 1] by considering arbitrary number
of points in [0, 1]. If we take only one point in [0, 1], then
S = {B(a, t, ϵ) :
all a, t, ϵ}
is no longer a topological basis. The topological basis generated by S consists of subsets of the form
B(a1, t1, ϵ1) ∩· · · ∩B(an, tn, ϵn)
where n is arbitrary. The topological basis in Example 4.4 is diﬀerent from this. However, we will see that the
two topological bases will produce the same topological theory on C[0, 1].
Exercise 4.7 Find topological subbases that induce the topological bases B2 and B3 in Example 4.1.
Exercise 4.8 Show that the subbasis S = {(a, b) × R : a < b} ∪{R × (c, d) : c < d} induces the topological
basis B1 in Example 4.2.
Exercise 4.9 For any integer n > 0, let Sn = {kn : k ∈Z} be the set of all multiples of n. For example,
S2 is the set of all even numbers. Describe the topological basis on Z induced by the subbasis S = {Sp :
p is a prime number}.

4.2
Open Subset
As in metric spaces, we may introduce the concept of open subsets from a topological basis.
Deﬁnition 4.3 Given a topological basis B on X, a subset U of X is open if it has the following
property
x ∈U
=⇒
x ∈B ⊂U
for some B ∈B.

Figure 36: deﬁnition of open subset
Example 4.8 We would like to determine whether (0, 1) is an open subset with respect to some of the topo-
logical bases in Example 4.1.
B1: For any x ∈(0, 1), we have x ∈B ⊂(0, 1) by choosing B = (0, 1) ∈B1. Therefore (0, 1) is open.
B2: For any x ∈(0, 1), we have x ∈B ⊂(0, 1) by choosing B = [x, 1) ∈B2. Therefore (0, 1) is open.
B5: We cannot never ﬁnd B ∈B5, such that x ∈B ⊂(0, 1), because any such B would be an inﬁnite
interval. Consequently, (0, 1) is not open.
Example 4.9 On the left of Figure 37, we have an open triangle plus one open (meaning: excluding end
points) edge. This is open with respect to the topological basis B4 in Example 4.2. In the middle, we change
the location of the edge and the subset is no longer open. On the right, we change the edge from open to closed
(meaning: including end points) and ﬁnd that the subset is also not open with respect to B4.

Example 4.10 Consider the topological basis in Example 4.3. By
1 ∈{1} ⊂{1, 2, 3},

Example 4.11 In Example 2.9, we have shown that the subset U = {f ∈C[0, 1] : f(t) > 0 for all 0 ≤t ≤1}
of all positive functions is open with respect to the L∞-metric. Here we will argue that U is not open with
respect to the topological basis B in Example 4.4.
Let f ∈U and f ∈B = B(a1, · · · , an, t1, · · · , tn, ϵ), we need to ﬁnd a non-positive function in B. This
would imply that B ̸⊂U and therefore there is no B between f and U. We may choose a point t0 distinct from
any of t1, · · · , tn. By modifying f around t0, it is not diﬃcult to construct a continuous function g such that
g(t0) = −1, |g(t1) −a1| < ϵ, · · ·, |g(tn) −an| < ϵ. Clearly, g ∈B and g ̸∈U, and g is what we are looking for.

Exercise 4.14 Show that the subset U = {f ∈C[0, 1] : f(0) > 1} is an open subset with respect to the
