Figure 100: what surface do you get
2. The edges are grouped into pairs and assigned arrows;
3. The (connected and closed) surface is obtained by identifying all the pairs of edges in the
way indicated by the assigned arrows.
The polygon with indicated pairs and arrows is called the planar diagram of the surface. Note
that the planar diagram also gives us a CW-structure for the surface. The following technical
result enables us to study surfaces by considering only the planar diagrams.
Lemma 7.2 Any connected and closed surface is given by a planar diagram.
Proof: We use the combinatorial description of a surface S as a 2-dimensional simplicial complex
satisfying conditions A and B. We try to arrange the faces into a sequence σ1, σ2, · · · , σn, such
that σi intersects ∪j<iσj (the union of all the previous faces) along some edges. We do this
inductively.
Suppose σ1, σ2, · · · , σk have been found and satisfy the desired condition. Denote the re-
maining faces by τ1, τ2, · · · , τl. By the connectivity of S, we can always ﬁnd some σi and τj
sharing a vertex v. Let σi1, σi2, · · · , σip, τj1, τj2, · · · , τjq be all the faces with v as a vertex. We
note that p, q ≥1. Then these faces can be arranged in the cyclic way as described in condition
B. The cyclic way implies that we can ﬁnd a face σiα and a face τjβ that are “adjacent” in
the cycle. Then the two faces must share at least one edge. In particular, the intersection
τjβ ∩[∪j≤kσj] contains at least one edge. Thus if we denote τβ = σk+1, then σ1, σ2, · · · , σk, σk+1
still satisfy the desired condition.
Now for the sequence σ1, σ2, · · · , σn satisfying the said condition, we take the union of the
faces one by one, in the order of the sequence and each time along exactly one edge. For example,
in the triangulation of the torus T 2 in Figure 101, the intersections σ2 ∩σ1, σ3 ∩(σ1 ∪σ2), · · ·,
σ5 ∩(σ1 ∪· · · ∪σ4) are all just single edges, and the union we construct is along this edge.
However, σ6 ∩(σ1 ∪· · · ∪σ5) consists of two edges: One is between σ6 and σ5; The other is
between σ6 and σ1. In constructing the planar diagram, we have chosen the gluing to be along
the edge between σ6 and σ5, and the other pair is no longer glued together. Similar choices are
made when we glue σ8, σ10, σ11, σ12, σ13, σ14 to the previous faces.
Suppose after the k-th step, we get a disk B(k). Then all the edges in the interior of the
disk have been identiﬁed. By the condition A, the common edge(s) between σk+1 and ∪j≤kσj
must lie on the boundary of B(k). Therefore if we glue along only one such common edge, we
still get a disk B(k + 1). Thus we have inductively proved that the end result of our union is
a disk B(n).

Of course, the edges on the boundary of B(n) must still be glued together to form S. By

We introduce the following simple notation for planar diagrams: First we give names to the
edge pairs. Then we start from some vertex and traverse along the boundary. Our trip gives
us a sequence of names, from which we make a word by using “name” when the direction of
our trip is the same as the assigned arrow and using “name−1” when the direction of our trip
is opposite to the assigned arrow. The examples at the beginning of this section show that S2,
T 2, P 2, K2 are given by the planar diagrams aa−1, aba−1b−1, aa, abab−1, respectively.

is given as in Figure 102.

7.5
Cut and Paste
Diﬀerent planar diagrams may give the same surface. We may rigorously justify this by the cut
and paste process. Such process is the key technique in the proof of the classiﬁcation theorem
in the next section. In this section, we practice cut and paste with some simple examples.
Lemma 7.4 The planar diagrams aabb and abab−1 give the same surfaces.
Since aa gives the projective space P 2, by Lemma 7.3, aabb gives the connected sum P 2#P 2.
From Figure 72, abab−1 gives the Klein bottle. Thus the Lemma basically says K2 = P 2#P 2.
The fact was illustrated in Figure 88.
Proof: The following process shows how we start with the planar diagram abab−1 and manipulate
the glueing process and get the planar diagram aabb at the end. The manipulation is taken to
make sure that the surfaces represented by the diagrams are the same.

Figure 105: abab−1 is equivalent to aabb
Here we explain in detail what is really going on. The ﬁrst arrow means that the square
may be considered as obtained by gluing two triangles together along the edge pair c. In this
way, the surface is obtained by gluing two triangles together along three edge pairs a, b, and c.
111

The ﬁrst equality is simply another way of drawing the pair of triangles. We keep the ﬁrst
triangle and ﬂip the second triangle upside down.
In the second arrow, we glue the edge pair a ﬁrst and leave the other two pairs alone.
The second equality means that the triangle is homeomorphic to the square, with edge pairs
b and c unchanged.
The last equality simply renames the edge pairs b to a and c to b−1.

7.6
Classiﬁcation of Surfaces
The following classiﬁcation theorem shows that the list of surfaces in Section 7.2 is complete.
The theorem was ﬁrst established by Dehn15 and Heegaard16 in 1907.
Theorem 7.5 Every connected closed surface is homeomorphic to a sphere, a connected sum
of several tori, or a connected sum of several projective spaces.
Proof: By Lemma 7.2, a connected closed surface is given by a planar diagram. There are
two types of edge pairs in a planar diagram: opposing and twisted, as indocated in Figure 106.
The dotted lines indicate the other edges. In terms of the words, an opposing pair appears as
· · · a · · · a−1 · · ·, and a twisted pair appears as · · · a · · · a · · ·.

Figure 109: aa−1 gives the sphere
As for the reduction of the number of vertice, note that the identiﬁcation of the edge pairs
in a planar diagram forces some vertices in the diagram to be identiﬁed. For example, in Figure
110, six vertices are identiﬁed as v, and the remaining two are identiﬁed as w.
The cut and paste process in Figure 111 shows that if a vertex v appears at least twice, then
the number of appearance may be reduced by one. We repeat the process until the number of
the appearance is reduced to exactly once. Then we must have an adjacent opposing pair on
the two sides of v, as in Figure 108. Thus v can be eliminated and the number of vertices is
reduced by one. Repeating the process will eventually reduce the number of vertices to one.
After the preliminary simpliﬁcation, we try to simplify the diagram to become a product of
aa and aba−1b−1. Since aa comes from a twisted pair and aba−1b−1 comes from (two) opposing
pairs, this involves two processes.
1. For a twisted pair · · · a · · · a · · ·, we bring the pair together and form · · · aa · · ·;
2. For an opposing pair · · · a · · · a−1 · · ·, we ﬁnd another opposing pair b located as
· · · a · · · b · · · a−1 · · · b−1 · · ·. Then we bring the two pairs together and form · · · aba−1b−1 · · ·.

Figure 112: bring twisted pairs together
Now we turn to the second process. For any one opposing pair a, we ﬁrst need to ﬁnd
another opposing pair b, so that locations of a and b are as described in Figure 113. The claim
may be proved by counting the number of vertices. Since we have already eliminated adjacent
opposing pairs in the preliminary step, the pair a must separate the boundary into two parts
A and B. Since all twisted pairs have been brought together by Figure 112, any twisted pair
lies completely in A or B. Suppose any opposing pair other than a also lies completely in A
or B. Then any edge pair (opposing as well as twisted) lies completely in A or B. As a result,
any vertex in A is identiﬁed with vertices in A only, and the same applies to vertices in B. In
particular, there are at least two vertices in the surface. This contradicts with that fact that
the number of vertices has been reduced to one. Thus we must have at least one opposing pair
b of which one edge is in A and another edge is in B. This is exactly the situation described in

Figure 113. Then the simpliﬁcation in Figure 114 further bring the pairs a and b together to
from · · · aba−1b−1 · · ·.

Now the planar diagram is reduced to a product of aa and aba−1b−1. By Lemma 7.3, this
means that the surface is a connected sum of copies of P 2 and T 2. Finally we use Lemma 7.1
and the equality T 2#P 2 = P 2#P 2#P 2 (proved in Theorem 7.6) to conclude the following.
1. If there is at least one P 2 appearing in the connected sum, then (one by one) each T 2
may be replaced by two P 2. The result is a connected sum of P 2 only.
2. If P 2 does not appear in the connected sum, then we have a connected sum of T 2 only.
This completes the proof of the classiﬁcation theorem.

7.7
Recognition of Surfaces
Theorem 7.5 gives us a complete list of closed surfaces. However, two important problems
remain:
1. Is there any duplication in the list? For example, it appears that 2T 2 ̸= 4T 2. However,
this needs to be proved;
2. How to recognize a closed surface in an eﬃcient way? The problem is illustrated by the
Exercise 7.18 and the problem in Figure 115.
..................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
........................................................................................................................................................................
........................................................................................................................................................................
......................................................................................................
......................................................................................................
.................................................................................................................
.................................................................................................................
............................................................................................................
............................................................................................................
...................................................
..........................................
Figure 115: is this 3T 2
The answer to the question comes from two invariants: Euler number and orientability.
The Euler number can be computed from the planar diagram. For example, we consider the
surface given by the planar diagram abcdec−1da−1b−1e−1 in Figure 116. First we decide from
the diagram that there is only one 0-cell (i.e., vertex) v. Since we have ﬁve identiﬁcation pairs
of edges, the number of 1-cells is 5. Moreover, there is always one 2-cell in any planar diagram.
Therefore the Euler number χ(S) = 1 −5 + 1 = −3.
By applying the idea to the standard planar diagrams at the end of Section 7.4, we conclude
the following.
Lemma 7.7 χ(S2) = 2, χ(gT 2) = 2 −2g, χ(gP 2) = 2 −g.
This lemma and Theorem 7.5 further imply the following.

Corollary 7.8 Let S be a connected closed surface. Then
1. χ(S) = 2 =⇒S = S2;
2. χ(S) is odd =⇒S = (2 −χ)P 2.
For example, the surface given by the planar diagram abcdec−1da−1b−1e−1 must be 5P 2.

Exercise 7.21 Let v, e, f be the numbers of vertices, edges, and faces in a triangulation of a closed surface S.

2(7 +
p
49 −24χ(S)).
The number ([x] =largest integer ≤x)
H(S) =
1
2

7 +
q
49 −24χ(S)

is called the Heawood17 number of the surface S. Jungerman and Ringel proved18 that, with
the exceptions of K2, 2T 2, and 3P 2, it is always possible to ﬁnd a triangulation of S with H(S)
number of vertices. In the three exceptional cases, the minimal number is H + 1.
The Heawood number is a very important invariant that frequently appears in the combi-
natorial studies on surfaces. In 1890, Heawood proved that H(S) number of colors is suﬃcient
to color any map on S. He also proved H(T 2) = 7 number of colors is necessary for T 2 and
conjectured that H(S) number of colors is always necessary for any S. It is also not diﬃcult to
show the conjecture is true for P 2. Ringel and Youngs proved the conjecture in case χ < 0 in
1968. With the help of computer, Appel and Haken solved the conjecture for S2 (the famous
four colors problem). This leaves the case of Klein bottle. Franklin19 proved in 1934 that only
six colors are needed for maps on K2, while H(K2) = 7. Therefore Heawood’s conjecture is
true for all surfaces except the Klein bottle.

18Ringel proved the nonorientable case in 1955. Jungerman and Ringel proved the orientable case in 1980
19Born October 5, 1898 in New York, USA; Died January 27, 1965 in Belmont, Massachusetts, USA

Figure 118: a torus map that requires seven colors
Note that gT 2 and 2gP 2 have the same Euler number. However, gT 2 and 2gP 2 should be
diﬀerent surfaces (for g = 1, we are comparing T 2 and K2). To distinguish between gT 2 and
2gP 2, therefore, another property/invariant (in addition to the Euler number) is needed. Such
a property/invariant is the orientability.
The plane has two orientations, represented by a circle with one of two possible (clockwise
or counterclockwise) directions. Since a surface S is locally homeomorphic to the plane, there
are two possible orientations in a neighborhood of any point x ∈S. We may indicate any
choice of the orientation by a small circle around x with a choice of directions, and we denote
this orientation by ox. Note that for any path on S from x to y, we are able to move ox to an
orientation oy at y along the path.
..............................................................................................................................................................
x ........................................ ox
..............................................................................................................................................................
y ........................................ oy
...........................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
.................................................................................................................................................
S
Figure 119: moving orientation from x to y along a path
Now we start from an orientation ox at a point x and try to move ox along all paths all over
the surface. There are two possible outcomes:
1. No matter which path we choose from x to y, moving ox along the path always gives us
the same orientation at y;
2. There are two paths from x to y, such that moving ox along the two paths gives us
diﬀerent orientations at y.
118

In the ﬁrst case, we get a compatible system of orientations at all points of the surfaces. Such
a system is a (global) orientation of the surface. In this case, we call the surface orientable.
For example, the sphere and the torus are orientable. In the second case, we do not have
compatible system of orientations at all points of the surfaces. Such surfaces are nonorientable.
For example, the M¨obius band is not orientable.
...............................................................
.......................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................

...............................................................................................................................................

...............................................................................................................................................
................................................................................................... ??
Figure 120: orientable and nonorientable surfaces
The nonorientable situation is equivalent to the following: There is a path from x to x
(called a loop at x), such that moving ox along the loop and back to x gives us the orientation
diﬀerent from ox. In fact, we can assume the loop has no self-intersection. Now the region
swept by (the little circle) ox as it moves along the loop is a strip surrounding the loop. The
fact that ox comes back to become the opposite of ox means exactly that the strip is a M¨obius
band. Therefore we have the following characterization of nonorientable surfaces.
Lemma 7.9 A surface is nonorientable if and only if it contains the M¨obius band.
As a consequence of the lemma, P 2 is not orientable.
Exercise 7.22 Show that a connected sum S1#S2 is orientable if and only if both S1 and S2 are orientable.

of the local orientations when we glue the boundary edges together. Figure 121 shows that we
have incompatibility if and only if there are twisted pairs.

Therefore a planar diagram represents an oriented surface if and only if all the edge pairs
are opposing pairs. In terms of the word, this means that if a appears in the word, then a−1
also appears in the word. For example, abcdec−1da−1b−1e−1 represents a nonorientable surface
because d appears but d−1 does not appear. Applying this principle to the standard planar
diagrams, we conclude the following.

Lemma 7.10 S2 and gT 2 are orientable. gP 2 is not orientable.
Combining Lemma 7.7 and Lemma 7.10, we conclude the following method for determining
whether two closed surfaces are the same.
Theorem 7.11 Two closed surfaces S1 and S2 are homeomorphic if and only if
1. χ(S1) = χ(S2);
2. S1 and S2 are either both orientable or both nonorientable.

8
Topological Properties
8.1
HausdorﬀSpace
Deﬁnition 8.1 A topological space X is Hausdorﬀif for any x ̸= y in X, there are open
subsets U and V , such that x ∈U, y ∈V , and U ∩V = ∅.
The Hausdorﬀproperty means that any two points can be “separated” by disjoint open
subsets. Note that we may replace the open subsets U and V by topological basis.
....................................................................................................... ...........................
x

x
y
U

................................................ .......................................................
Figure 122: Hausdorﬀproperty
The Hausdorﬀproperty is clearly a topological property. More precisely, if X is a Hausdorﬀ
space, then any topological space homeomorphic to X is Hausdorﬀ.
Example 8.1 Among the four topologies on the two point space {1, 2} in Example 4.14, only the discrete
topology T4 is Hausdorﬀ. The other three are not Hausdorﬀ.
Example 8.2 Among the topological bases in Example 4.1, only B5, B6, and B9 induce non-Hausdorﬀtopolo-
gies.

Figure 123: metric spaces are Hausdorﬀ
Example 8.4 The Hausdorﬀproperty is deﬁned for two distinct points. What about three distinct points?
Consider a Hausdorﬀspace X and pairwise distinct points x, y, z ∈X. From the Hausdorﬀproperty, we
can ﬁnd three pairs (U1, V1), (V2, W2), (U3, W3) of disjoint open subsets, such that
x ∈U1, y ∈V1;
y ∈V2, z ∈W2;
x ∈U3, z ∈W3.
Then U = U1 ∩U3, V = V1 ∩V2, W = W1 ∩W2 are pairwise disjoint open subsets, such that x ∈U, y ∈V , and
z ∈W.
121

Example 8.5 We prove the following interesting interpretation: X is Hausdorﬀ⇔The diagonal (see Example
5.20) ∆(X) is a closed subset of the product space X × X.
The problem is to ﬁnd out the meaning that X × X −∆(X) is open. A point (x, y) in X × X −∆(X)
means x ̸= y in X. The openness of X × X −∆(X) means that we can ﬁnd a topological basis U × V of the
product topology, such that (x, y) ∈U × V ⊂X × X −∆(X). Then we translate the meaning of the statement
as follows:
1. In the topological basis of X × X, U, V are open subsets of X;
2. (x, y) ∈U × V means x ∈U and y ∈V ;
3. U × V ⊂X × X −∆(X) means U and V are disjoint.
The translation indicates that the openness of X × X −∆(X) is the same as X being Hausdorﬀ.

8.2
Connected Space
Intuitively, a space is connected if it consists of only “one piece”. To make the intuition more
precise, we study the meaning of the opposite notion: The separation of a space.
In the deﬁnition of Hausdorﬀproperty, we separate distinct points by disjoint open subsets.
The idea also applies to separation of subsets: Two subsets A, B ⊂X are separated if we can
ﬁnd disjoint open subsets U and V , such that A ⊂U and B ⊂V .
Next, we ask when X consists of two separated pieces. In other words, we consider the case
X = A ∪B. Then we have
X = A ∪B,
A ⊂U,
B ⊂V,
U ∩V = ∅,
U, V ⊂X are open.
Note that this implies A = U and B = V . Thus the condition leads to the following deﬁnition.
122

.......................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
.....................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................
A
B
U
V
..........................................................................................................................................
........................................................................................................................................
Figure 124: separating A and B
Deﬁnition 8.2 A separation of a topological space X is X = A ∪B, where A and B are
disjoint nonempty open subsets. X is not connected if has a separation. X is connected if it
has no separation.
Since A and B are disjoint and X = A∪B, we have A = X −B and B = X −A. Therefore
the openness of A and B is the same as their closedness. Thus the following are equivalent to
a separation:
1. X = A ∪B, where A and B are disjoint nonempty closed subsets;
2. There is an open and closed subset A ⊂X, such that A ̸= ∅, X.
As the opposite of separation, the following are equivalent to the connectedness of X:
1. X = A ∪B, A and B are disjoint and open ⇒either A or B is empty;
2. X = A ∪B, A and B are disjoint and closed ⇒either A or B is empty;
3. The only open and closed subsets of X are ∅and X.
The connectedness is clearly a topological property. More precisely, if X is connected, then any
topological space homeomorphic to X is connected.
Example 8.6 Among the four topologies on the two point space {1, 2} in Example 4.14, only the discrete
topology T4 is not connected. The other three are connected.
Example 8.7 Let X be an inﬁnite set with ﬁnite complement topology. Let A ⊂X is open and closed. Since
X is inﬁnite, one of A and X −A (which are closed subsets) is inﬁnite. In the ﬁnite complement topology,
however, the only closed inﬁnite subsets is X. Thus we conclude that one of A and X −A is X, which means
that A = ∅or X. By the third criterion for the connectedness, we see that X is connected.
Exercise 8.10 Is the topology on {1, 2, 3, 4} in Example 4.15 connected? Which of the 3 point subspaces are
connected?
Exercise 8.11 Find a separation of Rl. This shows the lower limit topology is not connected.
Exercise 8.12 Suppose T is a ﬁner topology than T ′. Is it true that T connected =⇒T ′ connected? What
about the reversed direction?
The most important example of connected spaces is given by the following result.
123

Theorem 8.3 Intervals in Rusual are connected.
Proof: Let ∅̸= A ⊂[0, 1] be open and closed. We may assume 0 ∈A and try to prove A = [0, 1].
Otherwise, we may use [0, 1] −A in place of A in the subsequent argument. Denote
a = sup{x :
[0, x] ⊂A}.
Our problem becomes the proof of a = 1.
Since 0 ∈A and A is open, we have [0, ϵ) ⊂A for some ϵ > 0. This implies a > 0.
Next by the deﬁnition of a, we have an increasing sequence xn, such that [0, xn] ⊂A and
lim xn = a. Since A is closed, we conclude a ∈A.
Finally, if a < 1, then by a ∈A and A open, we have (a−δ, a+δ) ⊂A for some 1−a > δ > 0.
Combined with [0, a] ∈A, we have [0, a+δ/2] ⊂A. By the deﬁnition of a, we have a ≥a+δ/2.
The contradiction implies that a = 1.
The proof for the connectedness of the other types of intervals is similar. One may also use
the properties of connected spaces in Theorem 8.4 to prove the other cases.
2
In the next theorem, we assemble some properties of connected spaces.
Theorem 8.4 Connectedness has the following properties.
1. A is connected and A ⊂B ⊂¯A ⇒B is connected;
2. Ai ⊂X are connected subsets, and ∩Ai ̸= ∅⇒∪Ai is connected;
3. X is connected and f : X →Y is continuous ⇒f(X) is connected;
4. If X and Y are nonempty, then X and Y are connected ⇔X × Y is connected.
Proof: We only prove the ﬁrst and the third properties. The other two properties are left as
exercises.
In the ﬁrst property, we consider an open and closed (with regard to the subspace topology
of B) subset C ⊂B. The connectedness of B means that we need to show C = B or C = ∅.
Since C ∩A is open and closed in A, and A is connected, we see that either C ∩A = A, or
C ∩A = ∅. Assume C ∩A = A, i.e., A ⊂C. Then we have ¯A ⊂¯C (the closure in taken in the
whole space X), and it follows from B ⊂¯A that B ∩¯C = B. Since B ∩¯C is the closure of C in
the subspace topology of B (Prove this! See Exercise 5.42), and C was assumed to be closed
in B, we see that C = B ∩¯C. Combined with B ∩¯C = B, we conclude that C = B.
In the other case C ∩A = ∅, we may repeat the argument above, with B −C in place of C.
Then we conclude B −C = B in the end. In other worlds, C = ∅.
This completes the proof of the ﬁrst property. Next we prove the third property.
Let A ⊂f(X) be an open and closed subset. Then by the continuity of f, f −1(A) is open
and closed in X. Since X is connected, we then have either f −1(A) = X or f −1(A) = ∅. Since
A ⊂f(X) implies A = f(f −1(A)), we see that either A = f(X) or A = ∅. This proves that
f(X) is connected.
2
124

Example 8.8 Since the interval is connected, the square, cube, etc., are all connected (as products of intervals).
The balls are also connected because they are homeomorphic to cubes. In fact, any subset of Rn sandwiched
between an open ball and its closure is connected, according to the ﬁrst property. Moreover, the spaces in Figure
66 are also connected, after repeatedly applying the second property to the union of spaces homeomorphic to
balls at various dimensions.
Sphere, torus, projective space, Klein bottle, and their connected sums are connected because they are
continuous images of the disk via the planar diagram.
Example 8.9 The invertible real n × n matrices form a subset GL(n, R) of Rn2. The determinant function
det : GL(n, R) →R is a continuous map, and has R −{0} as the image. Since R −{0} is not connected, it
follows from the third property that GL(n, R) is not connected.

8.3
Path Connected Space
Theorems 8.3 and 8.4 may be combined to give a simple method for showing connectedness.
......................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................................

γ
Figure 125: path connected
Deﬁnition 8.5 A path in a topological space X connecting x to y is a continuous map γ :
[0, 1] →X, such that γ(0) = x and γ(1) = y. The space X is path connected if any two points
can be joined by a path.
125

Lemma 8.6 path connected ⇒connected.
Proof: Let X be path connected. We ﬁx a point x ∈X. Then the path connectedness implies
X = ∪γ(0)=xγ[0, 1],
i.e., X is the union of the images of the paths starting from x. By Theorem 8.3 and the third
property of Theorem 8.4, γ[0, 1] is connected. Moreover, x is a common point of all γ[0, 1] in
the union. Thus it follows from the second property of Theorem 8.4 that X is connected.
2
Example 8.10 The sphere, torus, projective space, Klein bottle, and their connected sums are connected
because they are clearly path connected.
If we take ﬁnitely many points away from Rn (n ≥2), sphere, torus, projective space, Klein bottle, etc., we
still get a path connected space. If we take a circle away from Rn (n ≥3), we still get a path connected space.

Example 8.12 We may use (path) connectedness to show R2 and R are not homeomorphic. Suppose there is a
homeomorphism given by f : R2 →R and g = f −1 : R →R2. Then the restrictions f| : R2 −{g(0)} →R−{0}
and g| : R−{0} →R2−{g(0)} is a homeomorphism between R2−{g(0)} and R−{0}. However, since R2−{g(0)}
is path connected and R −{0} is not connected, this is a contradiction.

8.4
Connected Components
Let X be a topological space. A connected component A of X is a maximal connected subset
of X. In other words, A satisﬁes the following conditions:
1. A is connected;
2. If A ⊂B ⊂X and B is connected, then A = B.
One may similarly deﬁne path connected components.
If A, B ⊂X are connected components, then we have either A and B disjoint, or A = B.
The reason is the following: Suppose A ∩B ̸= ∅. Then by the second property of Theorem 8.4,
A ∪B is connected. Since A and B are maximal connected, we must have A = A ∪B = B.
For any x ∈X, consider
Cx = ∪{A ⊂X : A is connected and x ∈A}.
Then by the second property of Theorem 8.4, Cx is connected. In fact, it is easy to see that Cx
is a connected component. Thus we conclude the following.
Lemma 8.7 Any topological space is a disjoint union of connected components. Moreover, the
connected components are closed subsets.
The closedness of the connected components follows from the ﬁrst property of Theorem 8.4.
The number of connected components is clearly a topological property. In other words,
homeomorphic spaces should have the same number of connected components.
Example 8.14 Consider the topology on {1, 2, 3, 4} in Example 4.15. Note that {1, 2, 3} and {4} are connected.
Since {1, 2, 3} ∪{4} is a separation of the whole space, we conclude that {1, 2, 3} and {4} are the two connected
components.
Example 8.15 We have GL(n, R) = GL+(n, R) ∪GL−(n, R), where GL, GL+, GL−respectively denote
invertible matrices, matrices with positive determinant, and matrices with negative determinant. From Example
8.13, we saw that GL+(n, R) is connected. Let D be the diagonal matrix with {−1, 1, · · · , 1} on the diagonal.
Then M 7→DM is clearly a homeomorphism between GL+(n, R) and GL−(n, R). Therefore GL−(n, R) is also
connected. Now we further recall from Example 8.9 that GL(n, R) is not connected. Therefore we conclude
that GL+(n, R) and GL−(n, R) are the two connected components of GL(n, R).

Example 8.16 We try to classify the alphabets A through G by homeomorphism.
Consider the following properties.
• Pn: The number of points of x ∈X, such that X −x is not connected, is n.
• Pm,n: The number of points of x ∈X, such that X −x has m connected components, is n.
These are clearly topological properties. It is easy to see that E and F are homeomorphic, and C and G are
also homeomorphic. Then we examine all the P-properties the alphabets satisfy.

8.5
Compact Space
Historically, the concept of compactness was quite an elusive one. It was understood for a
long time that compactness for subsets of Euclidean spaces means closed and bounded. It
was also understood that compactness for metric spaces means any sequence has a convergent
subsequence. However, straightforward adoptions of these properties (and other related ones)
to general topological spaces are either not appropriate or yield non-equivalent deﬁnitions of
compactness. The dilemma was even more serious in the days when the concept of topology
was still ambiguous.
Since then, mathematicians have gained a lot of insights and settled on the following deﬁ-
nition.
129

Deﬁnition 8.8 A topological space is compact if any open cover has a ﬁnite subcover.
Please do not worry if you feel completely lost at the meaning of the deﬁnition. I experienced
the same feeling when I ﬁrst saw this. We simply need to unwrap the deﬁnition bit by bit, with
the help of some examples.
First of all, by an open cover of X, we mean a collection U of open subsets, such that
X = ∪U∈UU. We may also talk about open covers of subsets: A collection U of open subsets
of X is an open cover of A ⊂X if A ⊂∪U∈UU.

Example 8.17 Let X be a metric space. Then for any ﬁxed ϵ > 0, the collection {B(x, ϵ) : x ∈X} of open
balls of radius ϵ is an open cover.
More generally, suppose B is a topological basis of X and A ⊂X is a subset. If for each a ∈A, we choose
Ba ∈B, such that a ∈Ba, then U = {Ba : a ∈A} is an open cover of A.

X = U1 ∪U2 ∪· · · ∪Un,

i.e., the ﬁnite subcollection {U1, U2, · · · , Un} still covers X. For the compactness of a subset
A ⊂X, we have

Example 8.20 Any ﬁnite topological space is compact. The trivial topology is always compact.
Example 8.21 Consider the open cover V1 in Example 8.18. Although the cover is not ﬁnite, we can ﬁnd a
ﬁnite subcover V2. Similarly, the inﬁnite open cover V3 has the following ﬁnite subcover
V4 = {(1/n, 1] :
n = 1, 2, · · · , 10001} ∪{[0, 0.0001)}.
Although we have two open covers of [0, 1] with ﬁnite subcovers, it is a grave error to immediately conclude
that [0, 1] is compact. For the compactness, we have to show the existence of ﬁnite subcover for all open covers.
Now we rigorously prove [0, 1] is indeed compact. Suppose U is an open cover of [0, 1], such that [0, 1]
cannot be covered by ﬁnitely many open subsets in U. Then at least one of the intervals [0, 1/2] and [1/2, 1] of
length 1
2 cannot be covered by ﬁnitely many open subsets in U. Denote this interval by I1. Next we divide I1
into two intervals of length 1
4. Since I1 cannot be covered by ﬁnitely many open subsets in U, at least one of
the two intervals cannot be covered by ﬁnitely many open subsets in U. Denote this interval (of length 1
4) by
I2. We may then further divide I2 into two intervals of length 1
8 and keep going. Eventually, we ﬁnd a sequence
of closed intervals I1, I2, · · · , In, · · ·, satisfying (i) In+1 ⊂In; (ii) The length of In is 1/2n; and (iii) None of In
is covered by ﬁnitely many open subsets in U.
We choose one point xn in each interval In. Then the properties (i) and (ii) imply that the sequence {xn}
has a limit x. Moreover, the property (i) and the closedness of In implies that x ∈In for all n. Now since
U is an open cover, we have x ∈U for some U ∈U. The openness of U implies that there is N, such that
(x −1/2N, x + 1/2N) ⊂U. Then the fact x ∈IN and the property (ii) imply that IN ⊂U. In other words, IN
is covered by one open subset in U. This is in contradiction with the property (iii).
Thus we conclude that the original assumption that U has no ﬁnite subcover is wrong. This proves the
compactness of [0, 1].
Example 8.22 None of the open covers U1, U2, U3 in Example 8.18 has ﬁnite subcover. Each one is a reason
for R to be non-compact.
Although the open cover W1 in Example 8.18 has W2 as a ﬁnite subcover. The other open cover W3 of
(0, 1] has no ﬁnite subcover. Therefore (0, 1] is also non-compact.

Exercise 8.32 Show that {1/n : n ∈N} ∪{0} is a compact subset of R.

Exercise 8.36 Suppose T is a ﬁner topology than T ′. Is it true that T compact ⇒T ′ compact? What about
the reversed direction?
The compactness is clearly a topological property. More precisely, if X is compact, then
any topological space homeomorphic to X is compact.
Theorem 8.9 Compactness has the following properties.
1. Any closed subset of a compact space is compact;
2. Any compact subspace of a Hausdorﬀspace is closed;
3. X is compact and f : X →Y is continuous ⇒f(X) is compact;
4. If X is compact, Y is Hausdorﬀ, then any bijective continuous map f : X →Y is a
homeomorphism;
5. If X and Y are nonempty, then X and Y are compact ⇔X × Y is compact;
6. A compact subset of a metric space is bounded;
7. A subset of Rn is compact ⇔the subset is bounded and closed.
Proof: We prove the ﬁrst, the second, the third, and the ﬁfth properties. The other properties
are left as exercises.
Let X be compact and A ⊂X be closed. Let U be a collection of open subsets of X covering
A. Then U ∪{X −A} is an open cover of X. Since X is compact, we have U1, U2, · · · , Un ∈
U ∪{X −A} covering X. If we delete X −A from the collection {U1, U2, · · · , Un}, then we get
a ﬁnite subcollection of U that covers A. This completes the proof of the ﬁrst property.
Let X be Hausdorﬀand A ⊂X be closed. We would like to show X −A is open. Suppose
x ∈X −A. Then for any a ∈A, we have x ̸= a. Since X is Hausdorﬀ, we have disjoint
open subsets Ua and Va, such that x ∈Ua and a ∈Va. By applying the compactness of A to
the open cover {Ua : a ∈A} of A, we ﬁnd a ﬁnite subcover {Ua1, Ua2, · · · , Uan} of A. Then
A ⊂Ua1 ∪Ua2 ∪· · ·∪Uan is disjoint from V = Va1 ∩Va2 ∩· · ·∩Van. Thus we have x ∈V ⊂X −A
for some open subset V . See Figure 130. This proves the openness of X −A, and completes
the proof of the second property.
Let X be compact and f : X →Y be continuous. Let U be an open cover of f(X). Then
by the continuity of f, {f −1(U) : U ∈U} is an open cover of X. Since X is compact, we have a
ﬁnite subcover {f −1(U1), f −1(U2), · · · , f −1(Un)} of X. This implies {U1, U2, · · · , Un} is a ﬁnite
subcover of f(X) and completes the proof of the third property.

Finally, the ⇐direction of the ﬁfth property follows from the third property and the conti-
nuity of the projections. For the ⇒direction, let X and Y be compact and W be an open cover
of X × Y . We ﬁrst claim the following: For any x ∈X, there is an open U(x) ⊂X, such that
x ∈U(x) and U(x) × Y is covered by ﬁnitely many open subsets in W. To prove the claim, we
ﬁx x ∈X and note that for any y ∈Y , there is Wy ∈W, such that (x, y) ∈Wy. Since Wy is
open, we have open Uy ⊂X and Vy ⊂Y , such that (x, y) ∈Uy × Vy ⊂Wy. Then the collection
{Vy : y ∈Y } is an open cover of Y . Since Y is compact, we conclude that there are ﬁnitely many
Vy1, Vy2, · · · , Vyn covering Y . Let U(x) = Uy1∩Uy2∩· · ·∩Uyn. Then U(x)×Vyi ⊂Uyi×Vyi ⊂Wyi,
and U(x) × Y = (U(x) × Vy1) ∪(U(x) × Vy2) ∪· · · ∪(U(x) × Vyn) ⊂Wy1 ∪Wy2 ∪· · · ∪Wyn.

Now for each x ∈X, we ﬁnd an open U(x) ⊂X as described in the claim. Then {U(x) : x ∈
X} is an open cover of X. Since X is compact, we have ﬁnitely many U(x1), U(x2), · · · , U(xk)
covering X. It then follows from X × Y = (U(x1) × Y ) ∪(U(x2) × Y ) ∪· · · ∪(U(xk) × Y ) and
each U(xj) × Y being covered by ﬁnitely many open subsets in W, that X × Y is covered by
ﬁnitely many open subsets in W. This completes the proof of the compactness of X × Y .
2
Example 8.23 The spheres are compact because it is a bounded and closed subset of Rn. As continuous
images of the spheres, the real and complex projective spaces are also compact.
The torus and the Klein bottle are compact because they are continuous images of the closed square, which,
as the product of two [0, 1] with itself, is compact. By similar reason, we may conclude the compactness of
many other surfaces.
133

Example 8.24 Consider the following subsets of R3:
A1
=
{(x1, x2, x3) :
x4
1 + x4
2 + 2x4
3 = 1}
A2
=
{(x1, x2, x3) :
x4
1 + x4
2 + 2x4
3 ≤1}
A3
=
{(x1, x2, x3) :
x4
1 + x4
2 + 2x4
3 < 1}
A4
=
{(x1, x2, x3) :
x4
1 + x4
2 + 2x4
3 ≥1}
Let f(x1, x2, x3) = x4
1 + x4
2 + 2x4
3. Then we have A1 = f −1{1}, A2 = f −1[0, 1]. By the continuity of f, A1 and
A2 are closed. Clearly, the points in A1 and A2 satisfy |x1| ≤1, |x2| ≤1, and |x3| ≤1. Therefore A1 and A2
are are also bounded. By the seventh property of Theorem 8.9, we conclude that A1 and A2 are compact.
On the other hand, we see from A3 = f −1(−1, 1) that A3 is open. If A3 were closed, then by the connect-
edness of R3, A3 must be either empty or the whole R3. Since this is not the case, we see that A3 is not closed.
Consequently, A3 is not compact.
Finally, A4 is not compact because it is clearly not bounded.
Example 8.25 As a subset of Rn2, GL(n, R) (see Example 8.9) is not compact, because it is unbounded.
On the other hand, the orthogonal matrices O(n) = f −1(I), where f(M) = M T M is a continuous map
from n × n matrices to n × n matrices. Therefore O(n) is a closed subset of Rn2. Since the columns of an
orthogonal matrix are vectors of length 1, O(n) is bounded. Thus we conclude that O(n) is compact.
